This is part of why the seventeen-issue run feels emotionally undernourished even at its best: the team is metabolizing every loss by sublimation into operational tempo, and the losses accumulate as unprocessed sediment that contributes to the terminal dissolution.

---

## VIII. VICTORY

Victory at the Champions is celebrated *insufficiently*. The team has a recognizable post-victory dip.

**Immediate post-mission (first thirty minutes).** Genuine warmth. Hercules will roar in triumph; Bobby will high-five everyone in reach; Natasha will smile (a rare event the file would weigh heavily); Warren will perform a victory lap of the airspace; Blaze will be Blaze again, exhausted, often kneeling.

**The hour after.** The energy collapses. The team becomes aware that it has nothing to do *next*. The press arrives, Warren handles them, the others disperse. The Coliseum or the office becomes a place no one quite wants to be alone in but no one wants to host in either.

**The evening after.** Hercules drinks heavily. Natasha is gone. Warren works. Bobby watches television alone. Blaze has left town.

**Decision habits in victory.** No structural decisions are made after victory. The team does not capitalize on momentum — does not announce next initiatives, does not raise funds, does not recruit. Victory is consumed rather than banked. This is one of the team's signature pathologies and is consistent with the avoidance-of-formation diagnosis in §4: a victory is a moment in which the team could choose to become more itself, and the Champions consistently decline.

---

## IX. DEFEAT

Defeat the Champions handle worse than grief.

**Immediate post-defeat.** Members do not look at each other. Hercules issues an apology in mythic register that is functionally a self-aggrandizement. Natasha says nothing and is mentally already writing the after-action. Warren apologizes to civilians, the press, and the city, sometimes in writing within twenty-four hours. Bobby's joke-rate spikes and then crashes to silence. Blaze isolates; he will not be reachable for two to seven days.

**The day after.** Headquarters is empty. Warren is the only consistent presence. The phone goes unanswered by everyone but Warren. If a follow-up engagement materializes in this window, the team will respond — barely — but the cohesion is visibly degraded; the distributed-command protocol slips and decisions get made unilaterally by whoever is loudest, which is usually Hercules and is usually wrong.

**The week after.** Reconvening is awkward. No one debriefs the defeat. The team performs *normalcy*, which is itself a tell — they are pretending nothing happened, and the pretense is mutually understood and mutually unbreached. This is the team's most consistent defensive maneuver: **the conspiracy of normalcy after failure.**

**Decision habits in defeat.** Defensive contraction. Warren tightens the budget. Natasha runs solo ops without telling the team. Hercules takes physical training to dangerous extremes. Bobby retreats to Warren-proximity. Blaze is unreachable. The team becomes, for a window of one to three weeks, *less* of a team than it was at baseline.

---

## X. CRISIS-OF-IDENTITY EPISODES (Mandate Challenge, Roster Threat, Public Disavowal)

A small but distinct register: occasions on which the team's *existence* is questioned rather than its tactics. Rampage's public framing of the team as oppressors; the Avengers' implicit non-recognition; civic criticism after the Coliseum loss; press cycles questioning whether the team is "real."

**Behavior signature.** The team becomes defensive in a brittle, over-articulated way. Warren writes statements. Hercules makes proclamations. Natasha refuses to engage and is therefore the most strategically sound responder — silence preserves optionality — but the team interprets her silence as abandonment, which it isn't. Bobby's humor turns acidic and is read by the press as immaturity, which costs the team coverage. Blaze does not comment, ever, in this register, which is the only correct PR posture on the team and is accidental.

**Decision habits.** The team will hold an emergency meeting (one of the few times it convenes formally), produce a statement (Warren-drafted), and disband for the day. No member proposes structural change. No member proposes dissolution. *The possibility of ending the team is never raised on-panel.* This is, retrospectively, the most diagnostic single fact about the Champions: a team chronically in identity-crisis that never once considered ending itself, because the unspoken function of the team — rebound holding-environment for five recently-ruptured attachments — would be lost, and no member is willing to be the one to name that.

---

## XI. UNDER SURVEILLANCE / KNOWN OBSERVATION

The Champions know they are watched — by press, by fans, by the Avengers, by federal entities, by their adversaries. Their behavior shifts measurably when surveillance is acute.

**Tells under observation.**
- Hercules performs *more* — louder, more sculptural, more mythic. He is incapable of being unobserved and observed


## Daily Life, Routines & Rituals

# DAILY LIFE, ROUTINES & RITUALS — THE CHAMPIONS OF LOS ANGELES

*A week of surveillance, reconstructed from the operational record (Champions #1–17 and contemporaneous appearances).*

---

## 1. THE PREMISES — WHERE A WEEK OCCURS

The team's spatial footprint, in descending order of dwell-time:

- **Champions HQ, a leased commercial property on a hill overlooking Los Angeles** (established in #5, design carried through to #17). Warren's money; Warren's lease; Warren's contractor. The building is a converted modern structure — open ground-floor common area, briefing room with a circular table, hangar/garage on the lower level for the team's quinjet-analog and Blaze's bike, individual quarters on the upper floor that are visibly under-decorated and under-occupied. Surveillance would record the building as **half-lit most evenings**: lights on in the common room and Warren's office, dark in two to three of the five private quarters on any given night.
- **The UCLA-adjacent corridor.** Warren and Bobby maintain student identities (carried over from #1–3 origin). Class buildings, the university library, a coffee shop favored by Bobby, a chemistry lab Warren never visits but is enrolled in.
- **Hollywood Boulevard and the stunt-lot circuit.** Blaze's residual professional habitat — he keeps contacts at two stunt agencies, a motorcycle mechanic in the Valley, and a costumer.
- **Romanoff's safe house(s).** She does not actually live at HQ full-time. Surveillance would identify at least one rented apartment, paid in cash, under an alias, in a mid-density Westside neighborhood where the foot traffic provides counter-surveillance cover. She rotates between HQ quarters and the safe house roughly 60/40 in favor of the safe house.
- **Hercules's drift radius.** Hercules sleeps at HQ when he sleeps indoors at all. He spends substantial waking hours on a circuit of wine bars, Greek restaurants in the older immigrant neighborhoods, the beach (Santa Monica, Venice), and — repeatedly — a particular hilltop park where he is observed simply sitting and looking at the Pacific for hours.
- **The bike, the wings, the ice-slide, and the chariot.** The team has no shared commuting protocol. Each member moves through the city by their own modality, which means the surveillance footprint of the unit is *five non-overlapping transit signatures* converging only at HQ and at incident scenes.

---

## 2. THE CIRCADIAN MISMATCH

A single chart of when each member is awake would show almost no overlap outside the late-afternoon to mid-evening window.

- **Warren** is the most regular. Up by 7:30, breakfast at HQ (someone — usually a contracted housekeeper, not a member — has stocked the kitchen), into the office area by 8:30 to handle correspondence with his East Coast financial managers (three-hour time difference; the calls have to happen early). Class or pretense of class mid-morning. HQ administrative work in the afternoon. Flying — *actual* flying, for exercise and to clear his head — at dusk, almost daily, an observable ritual. He files reports, signs checks, reviews the lease, talks to the publicist. He is the only member with a calendar.
- **Bobby** runs on a student's clock with a teenager's drift. Up around 9:30 if he has class, past noon if he doesn't. Eats constantly (his metabolic load is real). Comes and goes from HQ via ice-slide, which leaves a brief, very photogenic vapor trail observable from neighboring properties — neighbors have complained twice on the public record per the run.
- **Natasha** is on operational time, which is to say: she is awake when she needs to be and asleep in disciplined two-to-four-hour blocks otherwise. Surveillance would record her at HQ in late afternoons and during briefings, then *gone* — not in her quarters, not on premises — by 11 p.m. most nights. She runs at dawn, a five-to-seven-mile loop from the safe house through residential streets she varies on a rotation no one else has noticed. She practices on a heavy bag in a rented gym, not at HQ.
- **Hercules** does not have a circadian rhythm in any human sense. He sleeps when bored, wakes when hungry or thirsty, and is functionally available 24 hours a day, which the team learns to exploit and then to resent. Surveillance would record him asleep on the common-room couch as often as in his quarters. He eats enormous breakfasts at a particular diner in Hollywood at unpredictable hours, always paying in cash Warren has given him because he does not understand checks.
- **Blaze** is nocturnal by temperament and by condition. He is most often at HQ between roughly 2 p.m. and 8 p.m., and on the road or on a lot the rest of the time. He sleeps poorly; lights in his quarters are observed on at 3 a.m. with regularity. The Zarathos presence does not respect sleep cycles, and Blaze has learned not to fight insomnia he cannot win.

**The convergence window** — when all five are reliably in the same building — is approximately **5:30 to 8:00 p.m.**, three to four days a week. This is when briefings happen, when meals are taken as a group, when the team is, briefly, a team. Outside that window, the Champions are a building with people occasionally in it.

---

## 3. RITUALS — THE OBSERVABLE LITURGIES

A surveillance file documents not just what people do but what they do *every time*. The Champions have surprisingly few group rituals and a great many private ones.

### Group rituals (sparse)

- **The post-mission debrief that isn't.** After an engagement, the team returns to HQ, peels off into the common room, and Warren tries to run a debrief. It lasts, on average, eleven minutes before Hercules has opened a bottle, Bobby has wandered to the refrigerator, Blaze has gone outside, and Natasha has begun a separate, quieter conversation with whoever is left. *The debrief is performed; it is not actually conducted.*
- **The Friday-evening dinner.** Warren instituted a standing team dinner early in the run. It survives, intermittently, as the closest thing the team has to a shared meal. Attendance averages three of five. The food is catered (Warren's account); the conversation is shallow and warm; no one brings up the week's failures.
- **The press appearance.** When the publicist books one, the team rehearses for approximately twenty minutes and then walks in and improvises. Warren takes the lectern. Hercules makes a declaration. Natasha says nothing on the record. Bobby cracks a joke. Blaze stands at the back. This is repeatable enough to be liturgical.
- **The arrival ritual at HQ.** Each member enters by a different door. Warren and Bobby through the front (students; civilian-presenting). Natasha through a side service entrance she has rekeyed. Hercules through whichever door is closest to where he last was. Blaze through the garage. Surveillance counting heads at the front door would systematically undercount the team by 60%.

### Private rituals (dense)

- **Warren's evening flight.** Roughly forty-five minutes at dusk, almost always the same loop — up the coast to Malibu, inland over the canyons, back to HQ. He does not invite Bobby. It is the only time of day he is alone, and it is plainly load-bearing for him.
- **Bobby's late-night refrigerator rounds.** Two to three trips between 11 p.m. and 2 a.m. He eats standing up, in the dark, in front of an open fridge whose interior light he has unscrewed. He thinks about his parents. He does not talk about this.
- **Natasha's dawn run, weapons clean, and shower.** Invariant. The weapons clean follows the run. The shower follows the clean. She drinks a single cup of black tea afterward. She has not eaten breakfast on a recorded day in the run.
- **Hercules's first wine of the day.** Taken with breakfast, whatever the hour. He pours from a personal amphora he had shipped from Greece (visible in panels in #4 and again in #11). He toasts no one in particular. He drinks it like coffee.
- **Blaze's pre-sleep prayer.** Inconsistent in form, consistent in occurrence. He kneels, briefly, by his bed. He does not say what he asks for. Roxanne, when present, has noted it; the team has not.
- **Natasha's nightly counter-surveillance check** of her safe house perimeter. Four points, in the same order, every night. If the order is broken she does the check twice.

---

## 4. THE WEEK — A SURVEILLANCE LOG, RECONSTRUCTED

A representative week, mid-run (between major arcs; no active emergency):

### Monday

- 06:00 — Romanoff exits safe house, runs westward loop, 6.2 miles, returns 06:48.
- 07:30 — Warren wakes at HQ, breakfast on the terrace, sun on the wings (he extends them in the morning; it is a stretch, not a display).
- 08:15 — Hercules returns to HQ from somewhere unidentified, possibly the beach, sand in his sandals, smelling of wine, in good cheer.
- 09:00 — Warren on the phone with Worthington Industries' Manhattan office. Forty-minute call. Stock matters; he is functionally still a board figure.
- 09:45 — Bobby leaves HQ for UCLA via ice-slide. A neighbor's car has a frost-ring on its hood that does not melt for an hour.
- 11:00 — Blaze at a stunt lot in Burbank, consulting on a bike sequence. He is paid in cash.
- 13:30 — Romanoff arrives at HQ, reviews the previous week's incident reports Warren has prepared. She corrects two of his tactical reconstructions in pencil. He will not notice the corrections until Wednesday.
- 17:00 — Convergence. Four of five at HQ (Blaze still in Burbank). The publicist visits. A press request is discussed. Hercules wants to do it. Warren wants to schedule it. Natasha vetoes the venue. Bobby is on the floor playing with the dog (the building has a dog; no one will admit whose it is).
- 19:30 — Catered dinner. Three present.
- 22:00 — Blaze returns, eats reheated food alone in the kitchen.
- 23:30 — Lights out in three of five quarters. Warren reading in bed. Blaze's light on. Natasha gone — back to the safe house by 00:15.

### Tuesday

- Identical morning rhythms.
- 10:30 — Warren at the bank in person; a wire transfer of HQ operating funds. He is recognized; he signs an autograph for a teller.
- 14:00 — Hercules picks a fight at a longshoreman's bar in San Pedro, breaks no laws but breaks two tables. Bar owner is paid by Warren's office Wednesday morning, no questions asked. This is the third such incident in the run.
- 16:00 — Team training session, scheduled. Two members show: Bobby, Warren. They spar listlessly for thirty minutes and quit. Hercules is asleep on the couch through it. Romanoff is at her gym. Blaze does not train; he rides.
- 20:00 — A minor incident downtown — a robbery in progress reported on the police band Warren has installed. Three of five respond (Bobby, Warren, Hercules; Natasha unreachable; Blaze en route by the time they arrive). Resolved in nine minutes. No debrief on return.

### Wednesday

- 07:00 — Romanoff's run is shortened; she suspects she is being followed and confirms she is not. She does the run again that evening to verify the route is clean.
- 11:00 — Bobby skips class to go to the beach. He builds an ice sculpture, melts it, builds another. He is twenty-one and lonely.
- 15:00 — Blaze's bike is in for service. He is at HQ for an unusually long stretch. He plays cards with Hercules. Hercules wins; Blaze does not care.
- 18:00 — Friday-dinner standing has been moved to Wednesday this week (Warren's note; a conflict on Friday). Four of five attend. Conversation is, briefly, real — Hercules tells a story about his mother that the others have not heard. Natasha laughs once, audibly, which surveillance would flag as anomalous.
- 22:30 — A magazine reporter is on the perimeter without a credential. Romanoff identifies, intercepts, and dismisses her without raising her voice. Warren is not told.

### Thursday

- A "slow day." No incidents. The team disperses by 11 a.m.
- Warren visits his attorney (matters relating to the HQ lease and a pending lawsuit from a property-damage claim two months prior).
- Bobby spends seven hours at the coffee shop pretending to study, mostly talking to a girl who is not interested.
- Hercules takes the bus. He does not need to take the bus. He likes it.
- Natasha conducts what surveillance would record as a *meet* — an old contact, identity unconfirmed, a 22-minute conversation on a bench at Echo Park, no exchange of objects visible. This is not team business.
- Blaze rides Mulholland for two hours at speed. Late dusk; the Zarathos presence is closer than usual; he comes home shaking and tells no one.
- 23:00 — HQ is dark except for the kitchen, where Bobby is at the open fridge.

### Friday

- 06:00 — Romanoff's run.
- 09:00 — Sentinel-related intelligence comes in via Warren's old X-Men back-channel. Team is summoned. *This is the only moment in the week when all five are in the same room simultaneously, in costume, before noon.* Surveillance would clock the assembly time at 41 minutes from first call.
- 10:30 — Tactical planning. Warren whiteboards. Romanoff redirects the plan three times, twice silently (by simply being in a different position than Warren has placed her on the board) and once verbally. Hercules wants to attack at once. Bobby is afraid and hiding it with jokes. Blaze is calm in a way that registers, on close watch, as dissociative.
- 14:00 — Deployment. The fight occurs off-premises and is concluded by 17:20.
- 18:00 — Return to HQ. *No debrief.* Hercules opens wine. Warren is in his office on the phone with the property owner whose roof was damaged. Romanoff is icing a bruise alone in the bathroom; she has not told anyone she is hurt. Bobby is in the shower for forty-five minutes. Blaze leaves on his bike at 19:00 and is not seen at HQ for thirty-one hours.

### Saturday

- HQ is essentially empty. Warren is at a charity function in Bel Air (obligatory; he hates them and goes anyway). Bobby is with a friend from his pre-X-Men life. Hercules is somewhere on the coast. Romanoff is unreachable. Blaze is on the road.
- 16:00 — A teenage fan rings the HQ buzzer. The housekeeper turns him away politely. He leaves a drawing on the step. No one in the team will ever see it. (The "for the common man" mandate, in microcosm.)

### Sunday

- A loose reconvergence in the late afternoon. Three to four members present. Hercules cooks — actually cooks, lamb on a spit in the courtyard, the one domestic activity he undertakes voluntarily. The meal is the warmest event of the week. Romanoff stays through it. Blaze attends. Even Bobby is quiet, in the good way.
- 22:00 — The week ends with Warren in his office writing the next week's schedule. He is the only person on the team who knows what day it is in any planning sense.

---

## 5. HABITS OF PLACE AND TIME — THE GRANULAR TELLS

- **Warren's checkbook lives in the second drawer of his desk, left side.** He writes between twelve and twenty checks per week for team operations. He does not delegate this.
- **Hercules's amphora is refilled by a wine merchant in Los Feliz** who delivers on Tuesdays.
- **Bobby leaves frost rings on every chair he sits in for more than ten minutes**, which the housekeeper has learned to wipe before they warp the wood.
- **Romanoff has rekeyed the side door three times in the run.** Each time without telling Warren. Each time Warren has discovered it and not mentioned it.
- **Blaze's bike is parked, when at HQ, in the same spot, angled the same way, keys never in it.** The keys are on a hook he installed himself; no one else uses the hook.
- **The common-room television** is on more hours per week than any single member is in the building. It is rarely watched. It is comfort noise; the team treats it as a hearth.
- **The dog** — a medium mutt named, by Bobby, something that changes weekly — is fed by whoever is closest to the kitchen at the time. Surveillance would record the dog as the most consistently attended-to inhabitant of the building.
- **No one answers the phone.** There is an answering service Warren pays for. Messages are reviewed by Warren in the morning. The team is, structurally, unreachable on a timescale shorter than twelve hours unless an emergency band is open.
- **Romanoff carries, at all times, a small fixed-blade knife in her left boot.** This is not a costume element. It is on her in civilian clothes. It is on her at the dinner table.
- **Hercules removes his sandals at the door** in the building. This is not a Greek custom; it is a personal one. He is observed barefoot at HQ ~70% of the time.
- **Blaze keeps a small photograph of his stepfather** in the lining of his jacket. He touches it before he rides at night. He does not know that the others have seen him do this; Romanoff has.

---

## 6. DOWNTIME — WHAT THEY DO WHEN NO ONE IS WATCHING

Surveillance becomes a moral instrument here: the team's downtime is where the *earnest mismatch* of §5 of the prior workup is most visible.

- **Warren** does the books, calls his sister, attends to his father's company at a distance, flies at dusk. He is happiest in the air alone. He misses the X-Men with a steady, low ache he does not name. He is twenty-three.
- **Bobby** is, without supervision, *a kid*. He goes to movies. He eats ice cream — a fact he finds embarrassing and indulges anyway. He plays pickup basketball at a park near campus. He calls his mother on Sundays from a payphone, never from HQ.
- **Natasha** does tradecraft for its own sake. She walks routes. She reads dossiers — her own, on enemies the team has not heard of. She maintains an apartment the team does not know exists. She visits Daredevil's coast exactly never, but she reads about him in the papers. She is the only member with a continuous interior life from before the team and into it.
- **Hercules** *enjoys himself*. This is, in a team of walking attachment ruptures, almost shocking. He drinks, he eats, he flirts, he swims, he wrestles strangers on the beach for fun. He is the only member whose downtime is restorative rather than depletive. He is also, paradoxically, the most alone — no other member has the temperament to keep up with his appetites, and he does not seek out the others off-duty.
- **Blaze** is in a slow-rolling private crisis the team does not see. He rides to outrun a presence that rides with him. He prays. He visits Roxanne when he can; she is on the East Coast more often than not. He is the member whose downtime is *most necessary* and *least restorative*. A clinician with eyes on him would intervene; no one on the team has those eyes.

---

## 7. WHAT A WEEK OF SURVEILLANCE WOULD CONCLUDE

A week of disciplined observation would produce the following findings, in declining order of confidence:

1. **The team is not a household.** Five people pay rent on, or use, a building. They do not live together in any sociological sense. Co-occupancy averages 30% of waking hours.
2. **One member (Warren) carries all administrative load.** Removal of this member collapses the operational tempo within one week.
3. **One member (Romanoff) maintains an independent life and operational redundancy.** She is the only member who would survive the team's collapse without disruption.
4. **One member (Hercules) is the unit's emotional center of gravity and its biggest single liability.** He is loved, he is unmanaged, he is constantly mildly intoxicated.
5. **One member (Blaze) is in untreated psychiatric and metaphysical distress.** The team has no protocol and no awareness.
6. **One member (Bobby) is the youngest in every meaningful sense and is being parented by the building rather than by any person.**
7. **The team's "common-man" mandate is not enacted in any observable daily behavior.** No outreach programs, no civilian liaison, no neighborhood presence beyond response-to-incident. The mandate exists on letterhead and in interviews.
8. **The team is functionally reachable only through Warren's answering service.** Response latency for non-emergencies exceeds twelve hours.
9. **Convergence occurs reliably only under threat.** Threat is, therefore, the team's organizing principle in practice as well as in psyche.
10. **The week ends as it began: with one man writing a schedule no one else will read, and four people elsewhere, doing the private business of being themselves.**

— UATU, observing the week.


## Relationships I — Family & Origin Bonds

# Relationships I — Family & Origin Bonds

**Scope note.** The Champions are a *team*, not a person, and therefore do not have parents, siblings, or children in the literal sense. But a group organism inherits its "family" in two ways: (a) the *origin bonds each member imports into the team* — the parents, siblings, mentors, lost loves, and dead they were trying to outrun, replace, or memorialize when they walked onto the UCLA quad in 1975 — and (b) the *para-familial structures the team itself constituted* — the X-Men as Bobby and Warren's lost parent-family; the Avengers as a sibling team the Champions defined themselves against; the Defenders as a non-identical twin; Daredevil as Natasha's most recent ex-domestic partnership. These are the bonds the Champions, as a unit, are made out of and haunted by. Each is mapped below in the surveillance register: history, longitudinal dynamics, trust gradient, failure mode, leverage points.

---

## 1. Olympus — Hercules's Patrilineal Inheritance and the Father-Wound Imported into the Team

**History.** Hercules arrives in Los Angeles in *Champions* #1 as a guest lecturer on classical civilization at UCLA — an exiled posture he has occupied repeatedly since the Lee/Kirby *Journey into Mystery Annual* #1 (1965) and *Thor* #126–130. The exile is structural: Zeus periodically banishes him from Olympus for impolitic alliance with mortals (the recurring complaint across *Thor* and *Avengers* appearances throughout the 1960s–70s is some variant of "you debase yourself with mortal company"). Pluto's assault on Olympus that opens *Champions* #1 — the forced-marriage plot against Hera, the abduction attempt on Hercules himself — is in family-systems terms a *patriarchal succession crisis at the in-laws' house* into which Hercules has been conscripted from his time-out room on Earth.

**Dynamics, longitudinal.** Hercules's relationship to Zeus and the Olympian court oscillates across decades between two poles: *contemptuous independence* (he will not be summoned; he will not abase) and *bottomless need for paternal approval* (he returns the moment recalled; he sulks when not). The Champions period catches him mid-cycle: recently expelled, ostentatiously slumming with mortals, available to be re-flattered by anyone who treats him as the principal in the room. Warren's money and Natasha's competence both function, unintentionally, as surrogate-Olympian validations — a wealthy patron and a respected court-equal, which is approximately the social ecology Olympus provides on its good days.

**Trust gradient.** Hercules trusts Olympus *unconditionally and unwisely*. He will believe Zeus's pronouncements against direct contrary evidence; he will defer to Hera even when she is actively scheming; he will, as in *Champions* #2, follow Pluto into a marriage ceremony he has not read. His threat-detection within the family is essentially zero. Outside the family, by contrast, he is a competent if hot-tempered combatant. This asymmetry imports into the team a specific liability: **anyone the team encounters who can plausibly frame themselves as Olympian-adjacent authority will get an unearned hearing from Hercules.** Pluto exploits this in the opening arc; Ares exploits it; later writers (Van Lente's *Incredible Hercules* run) make a career of it.

**Failure mode.** When the father-wound is activated, Hercules does not collapse — he *over-asserts*. The Olympian-prerogative bid for team leadership in *Champions* #4, instantly overruled by the others' election of Natasha, is the visible symptom; the underlying current is "I cannot bear another room in which I am not the named principal." His subsequent inability to clean-handedly accept Natasha's authority is not misogyny in any deep sense (his relationships with female Olympians cut both ways); it is a man whose father has just told him he is unworthy, refusing to receive the same message from a second source.

**Leverage.** Any operator with intelligence on the Olympian court — particularly on the Zeus/Hera marital current, or on Hercules's standing relative to Apollo or Ares — can manipulate Hercules trivially. A forged divine summons would work; a flattering "your father spoke well of you yesterday" would work better. The team has no counterintelligence against this vector because no other member has the cultural literacy to recognize an Olympian phish.

---

## 2. Roxanne Simpson — Johnny Blaze's Imported Domestic Anchor and the Unspoken Fiancée

**History.** Roxanne is the *Ghost Rider* book's domestic constant from *Marvel Spotlight* #5 (1972) forward: Crash Simpson's daughter, Johnny's childhood-to-adulthood love interest, the woman whose presence in his life predates Zarathos and survives multiple soul-sales. By the time Blaze drifts into the Champions' orbit (his crossover appearances in *Champions* #1–3 and his recurrent involvement through the run), he and Roxanne are engaged, cohabiting in a Hollywood-adjacent arrangement, and locked in the central tension of the early *Ghost Rider* run: *Roxanne wants Johnny; Johnny wants Roxanne; Zarathos wants neither.*

**Dynamics, longitudinal.** Roxanne is the relationship in Blaze's life that has *actually been negotiated* — she knows the curse, has confronted Satan-figures on Johnny's behalf (*Ghost Rider* #9, the soul-purification arc), and chooses him with informed consent. This is unique in Blaze's biography; every other adult relationship he has, including the Champions, is something that happened *to* him while he was distracted by his possession. The Champions period falls precisely in the window where the Roxanne relationship is most stable and Blaze is therefore most able to function in a peer team — and yet he is *least integrated into the Champions of any founding member*, semi-detached across the run, treated by Englehart and successors as a guest-star on his own team. This is not coincidence: Roxanne is metabolizing the relational energy Blaze would otherwise have to spend on the Champions.

**Trust gradient.** Blaze trusts Roxanne with the Zarathos fact, which he trusts no Champion with. The team operates without ever being briefed on the actual conditions of his transformation, the triggers, the containment protocols, or the soul-economy he is embroiled in. Roxanne has this whole file; the Champions have a logo and a phone number.

**Failure mode.** The Roxanne-as-anchor system fails by *insulation*: it stabilizes Blaze well enough that he never has to integrate into the team, which means the team never develops the protocols it would need when Roxanne is unavailable (which, across the broader *Ghost Rider* run, is often). The Champions inherit a member whose containment infrastructure lives in a separate building, off-roster, unbriefed.

**Leverage.** Roxanne is Blaze's single point of attachment failure. Any adversary who threatens her removes Blaze from the chessboard — either by drawing him off-mission or by destabilizing his Zarathos-suppression into a civilian-context loss of control. The team has no plan for this and no early-warning system for when Roxanne's status is changing.

---

## 3. Crash Simpson — The Dead Surrogate Father and the Origin Debt

**History.** Crash Simpson, Roxanne's father, takes in the orphan Johnny Blaze and trains him as a stunt rider. Crash's terminal illness is the precipitating event of Blaze's demonic pact in *Marvel Spotlight* #5 — Johnny signs with Satan to save Crash, who is then killed in the stunt-jump anyway. Crash is the Ur-loss of the Ghost Rider mythos: the father-figure whose death Blaze was willing to damn himself to prevent, and whose death occurred regardless, leaving Johnny with both the damnation and the bereavement.

**Dynamics, longitudinal.** Crash is dead before any panel of the Champions exists, but he is structurally present in every Blaze appearance as the *referent* of the curse. Blaze's relationship to authority figures across the *Ghost Rider* and *Champions* runs is filtered through Crash: he is drawn to gruff, competent, paternal men (Hercules, briefly; later, the various Caretaker figures) and is incapable of disagreeing with them cleanly, because the template for paternal disagreement was foreclosed by Crash's death.

**Trust gradient.** Crash is trusted absolutely and posthumously; the trust is inert (the man is dead) but operative (it shapes which living men Blaze will defer to).

**Failure mode.** Blaze repeatedly accepts paternal frames from men who do not deserve them — most catastrophically from the various demonic figures who present as gruff bargainers. Within the Champions, this manifests as deference to Hercules's pronouncements beyond what Hercules's actual judgment merits, and as inability to push back when the team's mandate is misfiring.

**Leverage.** Any adversary who studies Blaze's biography and presents in a Crash-coded register — older, motorcycle-or-stunt-adjacent, gruffly affectionate, asking a favor — has a free first move.

---

## 4. Warren Worthington II — Angel's Absent-Wealthy Father and the Inherited Money

**History.** Warren Worthington III is, across all his appearances from *X-Men* #1 (1963) forward, the *son of money*. Worthington Industries is the off-panel infrastructure that funds his costumes, his planes, his eventual X-Factor headquarters, and — critically for this dossier — the entire physical plant of the Champions. The Champions building, the legal incorporation, the press operation: all are line-items on a Worthington Industries balance sheet that Warren did not earn and his father did not actively bestow so much as *fail to fence off*.

**Dynamics, longitudinal.** Warren Sr. is, across the canon, distant, busy, occasionally proud, occasionally ashamed (the mutation discovery arc; the later inheritance-and-poisoning arcs in *X-Factor*). The father-son relationship is mediated almost entirely through the trust fund. Warren III's relationship to his own money is *guilty and compensatory* — he gives it away in large gestures (the Champions; later X-Factor; later still the Worthington Foundation) in part to convert inherited capital into earned moral standing.

**Trust gradient.** Warren trusts his father's *money* and is ambivalent toward his father's *person*. The money is reliable; the person is not. This shapes the Champions' financial architecture: stable funding, no parental oversight, no board, no fiduciary discipline — exactly the structure a wealthy young heir builds when he wants to play house without an audit.

**Failure mode.** Warren is performing *patrimonial penance* through the Champions. The mandate ("for the common man") is in part a public-facing reparation for inherited wealth. Because the mandate is psychologically *Warren's* rather than collectively generated, the team's mission statement is a private absolution dressed in democratic language. The other Champions ratify it without sharing its emotional logic, which is why no one but Warren ever invokes it under pressure.

**Leverage.** Warren Sr.'s health, business, or reputation is a chokepoint on Warren III. Any pressure on Worthington Industries — hostile takeover, criminal investigation, scandal — pulls Warren off the team, and pulling Warren off the team collapses the team's financial substrate within a billing cycle. The Champions are, in corporate terms, a wholly-owned subsidiary of a single distracted heir's guilt.

---

## 5. Kathryn Worthington — The Mother-Shaped Absence

**History.** Warren's mother is largely off-panel through the 1960s–70s X-Men and Champions runs, surfacing in later retcons and inheritance arcs. For the Champions period she is structurally a *missing maternal register* — there is no mothering presence in Warren's biography that he could import as a model for team holding-environment.

**Dynamics.** Warren's caretaking style within the Champions — paying for things, providing premises, *not* providing emotional containment — is recognizable as the parenting he received: material provision without affective presence. He builds the team the house his parents built him.

**Failure mode.** No one in the Champions does the maternal work — checking in between missions, noticing when a member is off, holding the relational fabric. The role is unfilled and unrecognized as missing, because Warren, the de facto founder, does not have a template for noticing it is missing.

**Leverage.** Limited direct leverage (the mother is too distal). But the *absence* is a vulnerability: a competent infiltrator (a Mystique-type) could fill the maternal slot the team is unconsciously hungry for, and would be trusted disproportionately quickly. Darkstar's frictionless onboarding in #7 is a pale shadow of this vulnerability — the team accepts a defector with essentially no vetting because nobody is in the role of asking the protective question.

---

## 6. William Drake — Iceman's Hostile-Conservative Father and the Suburban Shame

**History.** Bobby Drake's parents — William and Madeline Drake of Fort Washington, Long Island — are established across the Lee/Kirby X-Men issues and codified in subsequent retcons (most pointedly in the *X-Factor* and 1990s Drake-family arcs). William is conservative, anti-mutant in the casual-prejudice register, embarrassed by his son, and the source of Bobby's lifelong joke-as-armor presentation. Madeline is more protective but not protective enough to neutralize William's day-to-day disapproval.

**Dynamics, longitudinal.** Bobby's humor — the relentless quipping, the refusal to take meetings seriously, the cryogenic puns — is, across his entire publication history, a defense calibrated to a father who reads gravity as weakness and difference as failure. By the Champions period Bobby is in his early twenties, has been out of the X-Men's holding environment for the first time in his adult life, and is *more* defended than he was at Xavier's, not less, because the new team does not include adults (Xavier, Beast in older-brother register, Cyclops as elder-sibling authority) who can absorb his deflections and translate.

**Trust gradient.** Bobby does not trust his father with his mutant identity or his interior life. He extends the same operational distrust to authority figures generally and to formal team structures specifically. Within the Champions, this manifests as a refusal to take the mandate seriously — Bobby is the member most likely to undercut a solemn moment with a joke, which reads as charming and is in fact a chronic defense against being known.

**Failure mode.** The Drake-family pattern reproduces in the Champions as Bobby's *inability to be developmentally adult on the team*. He is younger here than he should be at his chronological age, because the Champions, lacking the X-Men's elder-sibling structure, return him to the developmental position his parents froze him in.

**Leverage.** William Drake himself is leverage — any pressure on the Drake home pulls Bobby off-mission and toward a family he both resents and protects. More structurally, anyone who positions themselves as the *disapproving father* register — gruff, dismissive, conservative — will activate Bobby's deflection routine and render him tactically less effective. Conversely, anyone who genuinely *sees through the jokes without mocking them* gets disproportionate trust; Bobby is hungry to be read.

---

## 7. The X-Men — Bobby and Angel's Lost Sibling-Family and the Unprocessed Diaspora

**History.** The original X-Men (*X-Men* #1, 1963, through the reprints-only hiatus of #67–93, 1970–75) are Warren and Bobby's developmental family of origin in a more functional sense than their actual parents. Xavier is the parent; Cyclops is the elder sibling; Jean is the sister-and-eventual-complication; Beast is the older brother. The team's hiatus — the period during which the book ran only reprints and the founders dispersed — is the precipitating event that delivers Bobby and Warren to UCLA in the *Champions* #1 student-life status quo.

**Dynamics, longitudinal.** Bobby and Warren are, when they arrive at the Champions, *diaspora children*. Their family has dissolved without anyone dying; they are in unprocessed familial grief that they cannot name as grief because the X-Men are merely "on hiatus," merely not currently a team. The Champions are not chosen — they are the first available family-shaped object. Warren and Bobby bring an intact sub-bond with them (their friendship is the team's only pre-existing relationship) and they bring an intact *expectation of family structure* that the Champions cannot supply.

**Trust gradient.** Bobby and Warren trust each other in the X-Men register; they trust the Champions in a much shallower, more transactional register. When *X-Men* #94 restarts the book with the new international team, Warren and Bobby are *not invited back* in the founding rotation — they are reabsorbed only later and partially. This second rejection from the family of origin overlaps with the Champions' run and inflects it: both are processing a recent unchosen exclusion from the family they wanted, while pretending to be all-in on the family they have.

**Failure mode.** The Champions cannot compete with the X-Men for Bobby and Warren's deep loyalty. The moment the X-Men are functionally reachable again — and across both members' subsequent careers (Bobby's X-Men return; Warren's X-Factor founding, which is *the original five reunited*) — the Champions are abandoned without ceremony. The retrospective embarrassment both men later display about the Champions period is the embarrassment of the rebound-spouse photograph: real at the time, mortifying once you went home.

**Leverage.** Any signal of X-Men reactivation pulls Bobby and Warren's center of gravity off the Champions. Xavier himself, as a paternal figure, can recall both with a phone call. The Champions have no counter-pull.

---

## 8. The Red Room — Natasha Romanoff's Manufactured Origin and the Phantom Parents

**History.** Romanoff's origin, as established across her 1964 *Tales of Suspense* debut and successively retconned through the *Daredevil* run, the *Marvel Fanfare* solos, and later Bendis/Edmondson stories, is the *Red Room*: a Soviet program that took her as a child, fabricated false memories of parents (including, in some tellings, a false memory of the dancer Ivan Petrovich as guardian), trained her in espionage, ballet, and seduction, and engineered her into an asset. By the Champions period Natasha has *defected* (the *Tales of Suspense* and *Avengers* defection arc), *married and lost* Alexei Shostakov (presumed dead at this point in continuity; later Red Guardian retcons unwrite the loss), *partnered with and been left by* Matt Murdock, and is drifting west in *Daredevil* #124–ish and the *Champions* #1 onboarding.

**Dynamics, longitudinal.** Natasha's relationship to *family* is to a category she does not believe in as ontologically real for herself. Every parental figure has either been a Red Room operative in disguise or has been revealed (across retcons) to have been a placeholder. Her relationship to *origin* is to a forgery. This produces a specific operational stance: she trusts *built* bonds (the ones she has chosen and tested), and is constitutionally skeptical of *given* bonds (parents, siblings, nation-of-origin). The Champions are the first team she joins voluntarily as an unattached adult; she is testing whether built family can be made to work.

**Trust gradient.** Natasha trusts the Champions in the field — her field-trust is fast, accurate, and durable. She does not trust them with her biography; almost none of the Red Room file is on the table during the run. This withholding is not paranoia; it is the professional habit of an operative who knows that biography is leverage.

**Failure mode.** Because Natasha will not let the team know her, the team cannot include her at depth, which means the team cannot be the proof-of-concept she came west to test. She concludes (correctly) within the run's timeframe that built family is not being built here, and she begins the disengagement that her subsequent solo and *Daredevil* arcs complete.

**Leverage.** The Red Room file itself is the most acutely dangerous leverage in the entire Champions dossier. Any adversary with access to her conditioning triggers, her fabricated memories, or her authentic origin (the latter being the hardest currency) can move her without her consent. Across her publication history this happens repeatedly (the Yelena Belova arcs; *Homecoming*; Edmondson's *Black Widow*). On the Champions, no member is equipped to recognize a Red Room–coded approach, much less interrupt one.

---

## 9. Matt Murdock — The Recent Ex-Domestic Partner and the Heartbreak She Carried West

**History.** Romanoff's *Daredevil* cohabitation in San Francisco (*Daredevil* #81 onward through #124-ish) is, immediately prior to Champions #1, the most explicitly domestic relationship of her adult life: she and Murdock share a home, a city, and co-billing on the masthead. The relationship ends with Murdock's return to Hell's Kitchen and to Heather Glenn, leaving Natasha geographically and emotionally adrift in California — precisely where the Champions then find her.

**Dynamics, longitudinal.** Murdock is the relationship Natasha most recently *tried being a normal person inside*. Its failure is fresh in the Champions onboarding. She is, in clinical-attachment terms, in acute post-rupture status when the team forms. She is not looking for a new partner; she is looking for a structure that does not require her to be a partner — and the Champions, with their non-romantic team-coded affection, are an exact fit for that need, which is part of why she stays as long as she does.

**Trust gradient.** Trust in Murdock-the-person remains high across all subsequent canon; trust in Murdock-as-domestic-arrangement was burned. She has learned a specific lesson — that being someone's partner and being his sidekick are easily confused by the partner — and she carries it into the Champions as a refusal to be subordinated by the male leadership claim, which is part of why she accepts the field-leader election in #4 without false modesty.

**Failure mode.** The Murdock rupture leaves Natasha *unable to fully invest in another civilian-adjacent attachment* during the Champions run, which contributes to the team's failure to develop relational depth. Her withholding is half tradecraft, half convalescence.

**Leverage.** Any reappearance of Murdock in the Champions period would pull Natasha emotionally off the West Coast. (It does not occur on-panel; the writers protect both books.) More tactically, anyone targeting Murdock can move Natasha across the continent.

---

## 10. Daimon Hellstrom / Defenders-Adjacent Kin — The Sibling-Team Across the Country

**History.** The Defenders (Hulk, Doctor Strange, Namor, Silver Surfer, and the rotating cast that by the mid-Englehart era includes Valkyrie, Nighthawk, Hellcat, and Hellstrom) are the East Coast non-team that the Champions structurally rhyme with: another not-Avengers super-team, also operating in the misfit register. The two groups exist as fraternal-twin formations, never directly antagonized but mutually referential in the wider


## Relationships II — Allies, Chosen Family & Mentors

# RELATIONSHIPS II — ALLIES, CHOSEN FAMILY & MENTORS

*A group has a relational footprint distinct from the sum of its members'. What follows maps the people, institutions, and ghosts that stood beside the Champions-as-unit rather than beside any one member alone — every figure who, by entering the team's gravitational field, became part of how the team understood itself. The list is shorter than it should be, and that shortness is itself the first finding.*

---

## 1. Laynia Petrovna — DARKSTAR

**History with the unit.** Darkstar enters the team's orbit in *Champions* #7 as a Soviet operative on assignment, defects under fire across the **Titanium Man / Crimson Dynamo / Darkstar** arc (#7–11), and is brought into the Champions' field operations essentially by acclamation. There is no vote, no debrief, no security clearance, no sit-down with Romanoff (the *one* member equipped to vet her). By #12 she is fielding with the team as if she had always been there.

**Dynamics.** The team's posture toward Darkstar is the clearest single demonstration of its onboarding pathology. She is treated as a found object — beautiful, powerful, sympathetic — rather than as a defector with a handler-chain, a code-of-conduct, and a withdrawal profile that an intelligence professional would document on day one. Romanoff, who *knows* what a planted asset looks like because she has been one, conspicuously does not run the trade-craft. The most defensible read is that Natasha sees enough of her own younger arc in Laynia that the professional reflex is suppressed by something proto-maternal; the less defensible read is that the team is so starved for legitimacy that any willing recruit gets fast-tracked.

**Trust / safety.** Combat trust extends to her immediately and is, by the available evidence, not betrayed. Biographical trust is shallow on both sides: she does not unburden, and the team does not press. She is, in attachment terms, a *parallel* member — physically present, relationally tangential.

**Failure mode.** Had Darkstar been a residual KGB asset (the standard model her file would have warranted), the team would have been compromised end-to-end inside a month. The failure mode is **negligence-by-warmth**: the team's affection for her substituted for due diligence. This is the same mechanism that will, in different costume, recur in nearly every subsequent ally relationship.

**Leverage.** Anyone who can credibly threaten Darkstar's family, her standing with the Soviet émigré community, or her status as a defector can move the team through her without the team noticing the movement. Conversely, *withholding* Darkstar from the team — by recall, by injury, by counter-recruitment — removes the only post-formation member they ever managed to add, and confirms to the survivors that the unit cannot grow.

---

## 2. Ivan Petrovich Bezukhov

**History with the unit.** Ivan is Natasha's lifelong handler, surrogate father, chauffeur, and conscience — the man who pulled her from rubble in 1941 and never stopped being on duty. He is *adjacent* to the Champions rather than of them: present at the Los Angeles compound, driving the limo, maintaining vehicles, observing.

**Dynamics.** Ivan is the only adult-supervision figure within the team's actual physical envelope. He is also the only person in the building who has watched super-teams form and dissolve before (via Romanoff's prior tours). He sees the unit clearly, declines to manage it, and reserves his guardianship for Natasha alone. The team, to its detriment, never asks him what he sees. He would tell them. He is asked instead to drive.

**Trust / safety.** Ivan's loyalty to Romanoff is absolute and is, by extension, conditionally extended to whoever Romanoff stands beside. The team benefits from this without earning it. Functionally, the Champions have a free veteran handler on payroll whose institutional knowledge they do not draw down.

**Failure mode.** Ivan's loyalty pathway runs through Natasha exclusively. If Natasha quits — and across her career, she quits — Ivan goes with her, and the team loses its only mature operational adult overnight. There is no redundancy.

**Leverage.** Threaten Ivan and you move Natasha; move Natasha and you destabilize the command spine. Conversely, **win** Ivan — earn his volunteered counsel rather than his hired silence — and you gain a quiet, accurate window into every internal fault line the team will not name. No one in the building ever tries.

---

## 3. Richard Fenster

**History with the unit.** Warren Worthington's attorney of record, who handles the incorporation of the Champions, the lease and refit of the Champions' Los Angeles headquarters (the converted manufacturing facility in the early issues), the press apparatus, the insurance carriers, and — implicitly — the labyrinth of liability instruments required to operate a private super-team within California's actual regulatory environment.

**Dynamics.** Fenster is the **load-bearing wall** the team does not see. Every public-facing instance of "the Champions" — the name on the door, the logo, the press release, the standing arrangement with hospitals to bill the trust for collateral damage — exists because Fenster drafted it. He is treated as staff. He behaves, correctly, as counsel.

**Trust / safety.** Trust is contractual and high. He is paid, he performs, he is discreet. None of the team confides in him; he does not invite it.

**Failure mode.** Fenster's relationship is with Warren, not with the team. Two convergent failure modes follow. *First:* if Warren's wealth contracts (cf. the recurring Worthington-fortune crises across *X-Factor* and *Apocalypse* arcs), Fenster's retainer ends and the legal scaffolding evaporates within a billing cycle. *Second:* Fenster has no fiduciary duty to the unit as a unit. Asked to choose between Warren's interests and the team's, he is professionally obligated to choose Warren. The team has never noticed that its lawyer is not its lawyer.

**Leverage.** Subpoena Fenster, audit Worthington Industries, or simply file enough nuisance suits to occupy his calendar, and the team's public-facing apparatus seizes. He is the single civilian most capable of being lawfully weaponized against them.

---

## 4. Regina Dane

**History with the unit.** UCLA-affiliated scientist drawn into the team's orbit during the Stranger / Sentinels material (late run), one of the very few recurring civilian contacts the team manages to cultivate in the city the team supposedly serves.

**Dynamics.** Regina is the prototype of an ally the Champions could have *built a city presence on* — a credentialed, embedded, sympathetic professional with institutional access. The team uses her transactionally, for the technical problem in front of them, and never extends the relationship into the standing civilian network the mandate would require. She is *the case* for what the team's "for the common man" charter could have looked like in practice; she is also *the evidence* that the team did not know how to do it.

**Trust / safety.** Mutual, cordial, narrow. She trusts them to be heroes in the moment; they trust her to be competent on the bench. Neither trusts the other with anything beyond the immediate task.

**Failure mode.** Episodic. Regina returns to her actual job; the team forgets to call. The relationship has no maintenance schedule and consequently no compounding value.

**Leverage.** Modest, but precisely calibrated: Regina represents the **counter-factual** the team cannot bear to look at. A hostile actor surfacing her in the press as "the civilian the Champions used and dropped" would prove Rampage's case in a single news cycle.

---

## 5. Roxanne Simpson

**History with the unit.** Johnny Blaze's fiancée and the emotional anchor that pre-dates and post-dates his Champions tenure. She is not a member, not an operative, and visits the Los Angeles compound as a partner rather than a colleague.

**Dynamics.** Roxanne is the only stable, freely-chosen adult attachment any member of the team carries *into* the team intact. The Champions' implicit posture toward her is wary respect mixed with quiet envy — she is proof that one of them has a real life outside the unit, and the unit cannot quite metabolize that proof. Blaze, for his part, compartmentalizes hard: Roxanne does not see the Zarathos episodes if he can help it, and the team does not see the engagement-side of his life if he can help it. The two halves of Johnny's existence run on non-intersecting timetables that he is the only person maintaining.

**Trust / safety.** Roxanne trusts Johnny absolutely and the team contingently. The team treats her gently, in part because none of them have the standing to treat her any other way.

**Failure mode.** The Blaze/Simpson relationship is, across the longer Marvel record, repeatedly strained to breaking by the Zarathos condition — a strain the team neither caused nor mitigated. The team's failure here is one of **omission**: they had a member in chronic dissociative crisis with a civilian partner in the blast radius, and they did not build a containment protocol that would have protected her. That this never produced an on-panel catastrophe is luck, not design.

**Leverage.** Anyone targeting Roxanne moves Johnny out of the unit immediately and probably permanently, with Ghost Rider damage in transit. She is the single highest-magnitude civilian pressure point attached to the team.

---

## 6. Daimon Hellstrom

**History with the unit.** Appears in the team's orbit as a guest-collaborator during the Pluto and underworld-adjacent material, the period in which the book is briefly negotiating the question of whether it is a super-team title or a supernatural title.

**Dynamics.** Hellstrom is the only consulting specialist the team retains for the metaphysical case-load that, given Hercules and Ghost Rider's joint presence on the roster, is inevitably going to occupy a non-trivial percentage of their work. He is brilliant, troubled, and operates at a register (theological-occult) that nobody on the team — *including* the Olympian and the damnation-vehicle — actually speaks fluently. The team treats him as a contractor; he treats them with a slightly older brother's mild contempt for their improvisation.

**Trust / safety.** Operational trust is genuine in the moment; biographical trust is unestablished. Hellstrom's own divided lineage (mortal/infernal) is something he does not share and they do not ask about.

**Failure mode.** Hellstrom is not a member and will not be made one. The team's failure is in declining to either (a) recruit him properly or (b) develop the in-house occult literacy that would let them work without him. They split the difference and remain dependent on a consultant who could decline the next call.

**Leverage.** A hostile actor with sufficient leverage over the Hellstrom-family theology (Satan/Marduk Kurios, Patsy Walker arcs much later) can deny the Champions their only supernatural consult mid-crisis.

---

## 7. Bill Foster — BLACK GOLIATH

**History with the unit.** Appears in the team's Los Angeles orbit as a peer hero of the city — a Black scientist-superhero operating out of LA's actual neighborhoods, with a day-job, a community, and a civic footprint the Champions never assemble.

**Dynamics.** Foster is, *in fact*, what the Champions claim to be: a hero for the common man, embedded in the city, accessible, credentialed, and trusted by the populations the Champions' mandate gestures toward. The team's posture toward him is collegial and slightly oblivious; they do not perceive that he is solving, individually, the public-facing problem they have failed to solve institutionally. He is invited into the occasional team-up; he is not invited into the standing operational relationship that his existence on the same coast would warrant.

**Trust / safety.** Cordial, professional, surface-level. Foster does not need the Champions; the Champions do not realize they need Foster.

**Failure mode.** A standing alliance with Black Goliath would have given the team an authentic civic interface and a credible answer to the Rampage critique. The Champions never make the offer. This is, in retrospect, the team's single largest unforced error in the ally column.

**Leverage.** Modest and indirect, but the comparison itself is leverage: any critic with Foster's name in their mouth makes the Champions look exactly as performative as the most uncharitable read of them.

---

## 8. Henry McCoy — THE BEAST

**History with the unit.** Visits in the late issues during the Stranger / Sentinels material, the friendly elder X-statesman checking in on his two former teammates now adrift in California.

**Dynamics.** Hank's posture is older-brother-with-concern. He sees Bobby and Warren in a unit that is plainly not serving them, makes no scene about it, but reads the room with a clinician's eye. He is the only allied figure across the entire run who treats the Champions with **diagnostic** attention rather than collegial deference, and his read — silent, mostly — is unflattering. Hank does not poach his friends; he leaves the door visibly open.

**Trust / safety.** With Bobby and Warren, total. With the team as a unit, friendly-but-skeptical.

**Failure mode.** Hank's eventual function in Warren and Bobby's lives — co-founder of *X-Factor*, decades of co-teaming — is the **alternative attachment** that the Champions cannot compete with. The moment the Champions dissolve, both members route immediately back to the X-Men gravity well via Hank. The team never had them; Hank always did.

**Leverage.** The mere existence of a healthier available team to return to is permanent passive leverage against the Champions' centripetal force. Hank does not have to do anything to exert it.

---

## 9. Charles Xavier (off-stage, structural)

**History with the unit.** Never present in panel during the Champions run. Looms over Bobby and Warren as the founding parental figure of their actual chosen family.

**Dynamics.** Xavier is the *absent superego* of the team. Half its founding membership measures itself, silently, against a school they have left and a mentor they have not replaced. Warren's "for the common man" mandate is, at root, an attempt to articulate a mission Xavier never gave him and which he has had to draft for himself. That the draft is thin reflects how much of his prior identity was supplied by a man not in the room.

**Trust / safety.** With Bobby and Warren, foundational and unexamined. With the team, nil — Xavier has no relationship with the Champions and would not, asked, endorse the formation.

**Failure mode.** Two of the five founding members are operating with a permanent comparative against an earlier, more coherent institution. The Champions can never win that comparison, and the comparison runs continuously in two members' heads.

**Leverage.** Anyone who can credibly extend a Xavier-channel invitation back to the Mansion can pull Bobby and Warren out at will. This happens, on schedule, the moment the book ends.

---

## 10. Crash Simpson (deceased) and the Mephisto axis

**History with the unit.** Crash, Johnny's adoptive father and the stunt-cycle patriarch whose terminal cancer drove Johnny to the original Mephisto bargain, is dead before the team forms — but his absence is the single most determining fact in Johnny's interior life across the Champions run.

**Dynamics.** Crash is Johnny's only experienced mentor and the team has, by definition, no access to him. Mephisto is the **counter-mentor** — the figure who *did* answer when Johnny called, on terms Johnny will spend the rest of his life trying to revoke. The Champions inherit the consequences of both relationships and engage with neither: nobody on the team asks Johnny about Crash, and nobody on the team accounts for Mephisto as an active adversarial party with a continuing claim on a member.

**Trust / safety.** N/A for Crash; *negative* for Mephisto, who is not a relationship the team manages because the team refuses to acknowledge it.

**Failure mode.** Mephisto is a standing creditor on the roster. The team's failure to map this is the same failure as the Zarathos-containment gap — they have a member whose body is contested property, and they have no doctrine.

**Leverage.** Any infernal actor with standing to invoke the original bargain can pull Blaze off the field mid-mission and the team has no counter. This nearly happens repeatedly across Blaze's solo title in the same period.

---

## 11. Zeus and the Olympian house

**History with the unit.** Off-panel for the run, but structurally present as Hercules's family-of-origin and the cyclic source of his exiles. Pluto and Ares — both Olympians — are the team's *first* villains, which means the Champions are born in the middle of a Worthington-funded Olympian-family quarrel.

**Dynamics.** Hercules's relationship to Olympus is the classic divine-prodigal pattern: periodic banishment, periodic reconciliation, chronic ambivalence about which world is home. During the Champions run he is in an exile phase, which is why he is available to lecture at UCLA and available to join the team. The team is, to a non-trivial degree, **what Hercules does between fights with his father**. None of his teammates understand this; he does not explain it.

**Trust / safety.** Hercules trusts Olympus to take him back and to throw him out, in rotation. He trusts the Champions to be there until Olympus calls.

**Failure mode.** The team's most powerful member is structurally a flight risk on a divine timetable nobody on the roster can read. When Zeus recalls him, he goes. The team has built no contingency for the absence of its Class-100 anchor.

**Leverage.** Any actor capable of provoking an Olympian recall — or, more subtly, of brokering a reconciliation — removes Hercules from the team without the team having any say.

---

## 12. Venus

**History with the unit.** The proximate cause of the team's formation: Pluto's attempt to force-marry Venus is the assault that converges the five founders in *Champions* #1. She is briefly present, briefly grateful, and then absent.

**Dynamics.** Venus is the team's **midwife** and the team never sees her again in any meaningful way. The implicit relationship — that the Champions owe their existence to having defended her, and she owes them a debt of rescue — is acknowledged on neither side. She returns to her own register; they return to theirs. This is the team's first demonstration that it does not know how to **maintain** the relationships its missions create.

**Trust / safety.** Goodwill, undeveloped.

**Failure mode.** A team that cannot retain a relationship with the literal goddess whose defense was its founding act will not retain relationships with anyone else either. The Venus precedent generalizes across the run.

**Leverage.** Negligible operationally; significant symbolically.

---

## 13. The Avengers (peer institution as ambivalent elder sibling)

**History with the unit.** The Avengers are the formation against which the Champions are implicitly defined. Warren's "West Coast, for the common man" framing is **a deliberate not-Avengers**, and the Avengers — through Hank Pym, Vision, and others — are aware of the new team and broadly tolerant of it. Cross-membership and cross-pollination are sparse during the active run; the relationship is mostly a long stare across the continent.

**Dynamics.** The Champions need the Avengers to exist so they can be the alternative; the Avengers do not particularly need the Champions to exist at all. This is an asymmetry the Champions feel and the Avengers don't. The eventual founding of the *West Coast Avengers* in 1984 — the team the Champions implicitly tried to be, done with adult infrastructure — is the institutional verdict on the Champions' attempt.

**Trust / safety.** Professional cordiality. Romanoff is a former Avenger and will be again; Hercules will be one later; the channel is open at the individual level and absent at the team level.

**Failure mode.** The Champions can be made obsolete by the Avengers simply doing the West Coast job correctly. They eventually are.

**Leverage.** The Avengers do not need to **do** anything to leverage the Champions. Their existence sets the ceiling on the Champions' legitimacy.

---

## 14. The City of Los Angeles (LAPD, Mayor's Office, civic press)

**History with the unit.** A relationship the Champions claim and do not cultivate. There is no Champions/LAPD liaison, no standing arrangement with the Mayor's office, no formal coordination with Fire or EMS, no civic-press strategy beyond Warren's publicist apparatus. The team operates in the city, on the city, but not **with** the city.

**Dynamics.** The civic relationship is precisely the one the m


## Relationships III — Rivals, Enemies & Lovers

# Relationships III — Rivals, Enemies & Lovers
## Champions of Los Angeles (Original Roster), as a Group Organism

A note on framing before the entries. The Champions are not a person; they relate as a *group* to other parties, and the texture of that relating is distinct from any single member's bilateral history. Where a foe, peer, or intimate engaged the team *as the team*, they are mapped here. Individual romantic histories (Warren/Candy, Natasha/Matt, Blaze/Roxanne, Bobby's serial dating, Hercules/innumerable) are referenced only where they intersected the unit's life. The relationships are sorted by category and within each by the depth of imprint left on group behavior.

---

## I. RIVALS — Peer Teams and Institutional Counterparts

### The Avengers (East Coast establishment)

**History.** The Avengers are the *unstated reference team* against which the Champions were constituted. Warren's "for the common man" mandate (Champions #5–7) is functionally a negative — *not them*, not the Mansion, not the government liaison, not the security clearances, not the Vision standing in the foyer at 3 a.m. logging incident reports. No founding Champion was offered or sought Avengers membership at the moment of formation; the team's identity is built in the shadow of an institution that did not extend an invitation.

**Dynamics.** Cordial in person, competitive in self-concept. When Avengers and Champions cross paths in the run and in adjacent appearances (Magneto-adjacent threats, Warren's prior team-ups), the Avengers behave like adults visiting a younger sibling's clubhouse — polite, faintly amused, faintly worried. The Champions behave like the younger sibling: too eager, too defensive, over-explaining the clubhouse rules.

**Evolution.** As the run wears on, the implicit comparison curdles. The Champions stop invoking the Avengers entirely; mention is felt as concession. Post-dissolution, every member who joins or rejoins an Avengers-adjacent formation (Natasha most centrally, Hercules eventually, Warren peripherally via X-factions that brush against Avengers operations) carries forward a slightly defensive posture toward Avengers institutional culture — not hostile, just *not at home* in it.

**Trust / Safety.** The Avengers are safe in the sense that they will not betray; unsafe in the sense that proximity to them collapses the Champions' identity. The team avoids extended exposure.

**Failure mode.** Any scenario requiring joint operations forces the Champions to confront that they cannot match the Avengers' command discipline, intelligence pipeline, or threat-assessment apparatus. Romanoff alone could professionalize the team to Avengers standard; she conspicuously does not, because doing so would erase the team's reason for existing.

**Leverage.** An Avengers offer extended to *one* Champion — particularly Hercules or Natasha — would, by hypothesis, split the team within days. The Champions' cohesion depends on no member having a better-rated formation to leave for.

---

### The Defenders (the non-team)

**History.** The structural inverse. The Defenders insist they are not a team; the Champions insist they are. Both are wrong in mirror-image ways. Doctor Strange, Hulk, Namor, and Silver Surfer (and the rotating mid-roster — Nighthawk, Valkyrie, Hellcat) demonstrate by negative example that *not articulating teamness* is sometimes the only thing that lets disparate beings cooperate. The Champions never absorb this lesson.

**Dynamics.** Geographically near (both West Coast–adjacent at points), philosophically antipodal. The Defenders look at the Champions the way ascetics look at celebrities: with mild pity. The Champions look at the Defenders the way credentialed amateurs look at unlicensed savants: with mild contempt that conceals envy.

**Trust / Safety.** Operational trust available on demand; relational trust nil. Neither team has the conceptual category for the other.

**Failure mode.** A crossover would expose that the Defenders' loose grammar handles the Champions' actual problems (power-curve incoherence, scale-mismatch, dissociative members) far better than the Champions' contractual grammar does. This is humiliating in prospect, so it does not happen.

**Leverage.** The Defenders' very existence is leverage; their presence in the West Coast hero-ecology is a constant reproach. The Champions over-perform their teamness in part because the Defenders are out there under-performing theirs and getting away with it.

---

### The X-Men (the lost holding environment)

**History.** Warren and Bobby's *prior* family. The Champions exist, on the Warren-Bobby axis, because the X-Men were in hiatus (post-Thomas, pre–Giant-Size). When the new X-Men reform under Wein/Cockrum/Claremont, the gravitational pull on the two founding mutants is immediate and unresolved.

**Dynamics.** Warren and Bobby relate to the X-Men as one relates to a parental home one has left without quite moving out. They do not talk about it much on-panel; they do not need to. The Champions, as a unit, register the X-Men's reformation as an existential threat — two of five founders have a *prior, better* attachment available.

**Evolution.** Both Warren and Bobby drift back X-ward after the Champions dissolve; neither stays put. Their post-Champions careers (X-Factor, *X-Men* runs, solo titles) treat the LA period as a brief secondment rather than a defining tenure.

**Trust / Safety.** The X-Men are safer than the Champions ever were; that is the problem.

**Failure mode.** Whenever an X-related threat lands on the team's table (Magneto-orbit issues, Sentinels in #15–17), Warren and Bobby effectively *become* X-Men for the duration of the engagement; the other three Champions become extras in someone else's book. The team's center cannot hold against this gravitational drift.

**Leverage.** The X-Men did not even have to *try* to pull two founders back; their existence sufficed.

---

### LAPD / Civic Authorities

**History.** Los Angeles in the mid-1970s has no established protocol for super-team operations. The Champions, headquartered in the city, become a *de facto* test case. The relationship is administrative friction from issue one: permits, property damage liability, jurisdictional ambiguity, press relations.

**Dynamics.** Warren's money handles most of it. The civic posture toward the team is wary, occasionally exasperated, intermittently grateful. There is no police liaison; there is no formal mutual aid agreement. Compare the Avengers' federal credentialing or the Fantastic Four's NYC carve-outs.

**Trust / Safety.** Low. The Champions are tolerated, not endorsed. One bad incident from public custody.

**Failure mode.** A Ghost Rider containment failure in a civilian zone, a Rampage-style PR catastrophe, or a Hercules drunk-and-disorderly with property damage would each, individually, end the team's local welcome. None of these is hypothetical; all three are *near-misses on-panel*.

**Leverage.** The city does not need the Champions; the Champions need the city. Asymmetric, and the team never closes the gap.

---

## II. ENEMIES — Antagonists Who Shaped the Group's Self-Understanding

### Pluto (Olympian, Lord of the Underworld) — the *natal* enemy

**History.** The team forms in the act of repelling Pluto's plot to force a marriage between Hercules and Hippolyta, and Venus to Ares, binding Olympian succession. Pluto is therefore not merely the first villain; he is the **obstetrician of the unit**, the figure whose attack converts five strangers on a UCLA quad into a formation.

**Dynamics.** Pluto reads the Champions as nuisance and Hercules as property. He never engages them as a team in their own right; the team experiences this as *erasure* and over-asserts itself in response. Hercules's bluster on subsequent Pluto-adjacent matters is, partly, performance for teammates who he knows are watching to see if he treats the Pluto matter as a *family quarrel* or a *team threat*.

**Evolution.** Pluto recurs as a hovering Olympian-politics threat; the team's response to him calcifies into "let Hercules handle it," which deepens the leadership ambiguity already corroding the Hercules/Romanoff axis.

**Trust / Safety.** None. Pluto is an active cosmological enemy of one member; the rest are collateral. The team has no doctrine for *one member's mythology being a standing threat to all*.

**Failure mode.** A Pluto operation aimed at Hercules in which the other four are bait collapses Romanoff's command authority publicly, because Hercules will not subordinate his Olympian business to her tactical lead.

**Leverage.** Pluto's standing claim on Hercules is permanent leverage over the entire unit. The team cannot resolve it; they can only postpone.

---

### Ares (Olympian, God of War) — the natal enemy's enforcer

**History.** Pluto's co-conspirator at the team's birth. Ares is, for purposes of the Champions' file, less a separate enemy than the *muscle* of the Pluto problem.

**Dynamics.** Ares engages the team head-on, treats Hercules as a peer-rival, dismisses the others. His contempt for non-Olympian combatants is total and produces the team's first shared experience of being *insulted as a unit* — an underrated cohesion event. The Champions bond, briefly and tightly, in the act of proving an Olympian war-god wrong about them.

**Evolution.** Ares' subsequent presence in the Marvel cosmology never returns to the Champions as a team; the relationship is sealed at formation.

**Trust / Safety.** None; not relevant.

**Failure mode.** A second Ares engagement, with the team older and more frayed, would not reproduce the cohesion bump. The unit's response to insult-as-unit was a young-team phenomenon.

**Leverage.** Minimal post-formation. Ares is a memory the team uses to remember it was once a team.

---

### Rampage (Stuart Clarke) — the **mandate's mirror**
