**History.** A small-businessman bankrupted by recession-era pressures, who builds power armor and acts out in a rage that is, by his own account, *aimed at the economic system the Champions purport to defend ordinary people from*. The team beats him in #5–6; he returns radicalized in #15–17. This is the single most psychologically loaded enemy in the file.

**Dynamics.** Rampage is the **stress test of "for the common man."** He *is* the common man — frustrated, dispossessed, technically gifted, morally legible — and the Champions cannot help him; they can only subdue him. Every engagement with Rampage exposes the cosmetic nature of the team's mandate. The team senses this and responds with extra force, which is exactly the wrong response and Rampage's radicalization confirms it.

**Evolution.** Across the two arcs, Rampage gets worse and the team gets more defensive about its mission. By #17, Rampage is no longer asking the team to live up to its mandate; he is treating the mandate as a lie. This read is *correct*, and the team has no rebuttal.

**Trust / Safety.** None on either side. The deeper problem: the Champions cannot articulate, even among themselves, what they would have done differently to keep Rampage from becoming Rampage. Their silence on this is the silence of an institution that knows its premises do not hold.

**Failure mode.** Rampage is the team's *signature* failure mode. Any "ordinary person" antagonist who frames the team as enemies of the people they claim to serve activates the same fracture and the team has no defense.

**Leverage.** Total. Rampage demonstrates that the Champions' mandate can be weaponized against them by anyone willing to live it more sincerely than they do.

---

### The Stranger — the **cosmological dismissal**

**History.** Cosmic entity, prior Marvel-cosmology resident, engaged in the Sentinels arc (#15–17). The Stranger treats the team the way the team feared the Avengers would: as specimens, not peers.

**Dynamics.** The Stranger does not negotiate. The Champions, who have built their identity on accessibility and warmth, cannot find purchase on a being for whom warmth is irrelevant. The arc forces the team into purely tactical posture — and the tactical posture is what they are *worst* at sustaining across a multi-issue arc, because their cohesion depends on the affective register.

**Evolution.** The book is cancelled mid-arc; the Stranger relationship is never resolved. This is fitting: the team's last antagonist is one for whom the team is not even a recognizable category.

**Trust / Safety.** Asymmetric to the point of non-relation.

**Failure mode.** Sustained engagement with cosmic-tier entities surfaces every weakness simultaneously — power-curve incoherence, leadership ambiguity, moral-epistemology fragmentation. The team is not built for cosmic.

**Leverage.** The Stranger does not need leverage; he has scale.

---

### The Sentinels (Mark II/III variants, late-run) — the **borrowed enemy**

**History.** Sentinels are X-Men antagonists by birthright. Their appearance in the Champions' run is a *borrowed* threat — pulled in because two of five founders are mutants and the late book is straining for relevance.

**Dynamics.** The Sentinels engagement makes the Champions briefly into a junior X-Men. Warren and Bobby move to the front; the other three orbit. Romanoff's espionage skill is wasted on robot adversaries who do not have psychology to exploit; Hercules is reduced to a smashing tool; Blaze's metaphysics are irrelevant to machine intelligence.

**Trust / Safety.** Sentinels do not transact in trust. The relevant safety question is between team members under Sentinel pressure, and the answer is: they protect each other adequately but they re-sort along mutant/non-mutant lines under stress.

**Failure mode.** Sentinels make visible that the team's "common man" mandate has no purchase on anti-mutant politics. The Champions cannot speak coherently to the public about Sentinel threats because the team's mandate did not contemplate identity-targeted persecution as a category.

**Leverage.** The Sentinel arc leverages the team's incoherence about mutant politics. There is no Champions position on mutancy; there are five private positions and a polite silence.

---

### Swarm — the **uncanny outlier**

**History.** Fritz von Meyer, Nazi-collective-consciousness-of-bees. A genuinely strange villain, engaged mid-run.

**Dynamics.** Swarm's threat profile (distributed body, no center of mass, fear-based crowd-control problem) defeats the team's standard tactical script (concentrate firepower on one body). The Champions improvise effectively; this is one of the run's better engagements.

**Evolution.** No long arc; one-and-done. But the engagement is filed here because it is *the one antagonist whose threat profile actually demanded the Champions' specific composition*: Iceman's area-effect freezing, Angel's aerial mobility, Ghost Rider's hellfire, Hercules's environmental destruction tolerance, Romanoff's coordination. For a single fight, the team is *the right team for the job*. The book never quite figures out how to generalize this.

**Trust / Safety.** Strong intra-team during the engagement; this is a high-cohesion mission.

**Failure mode.** The team mistakes the Swarm engagement for proof that the formation works generally, when in fact it works specifically.

**Leverage.** None; Swarm is non-strategic.

---

### MODOK / AIM — the **professionalization gap**

**History.** Engaged in the run; relevant because MODOK/AIM operate at the level of *institutional* villainy — labs, supply chains, doctrine.

**Dynamics.** The team can punch the symptom; they cannot dismantle the apparatus. Romanoff has the skills to run a sustained intelligence operation against AIM; the team does not have the structure to support one.

**Trust / Safety.** Irrelevant; AIM is faceless.

**Failure mode.** Institutional villainy reveals the Champions' lack of institutional self. They can win fights and lose campaigns.

**Leverage.** AIM-tier operations leverage exactly the gap between the team's tactical capability and its strategic infrastructure — a gap that is, properly speaking, the team's defining negative space.

---

### Doctor Doom (peripheral engagement) — the **scale rebuke**

**History.** Doom enters the team's orbit briefly; the engagement is dispositive of nothing but instructive of much.

**Dynamics.** Doom does not recognize the Champions as a tier of opposition. He engages them with subordinate forces. The team experiences this as humiliation; the humiliation produces no reform.

**Trust / Safety.** Not relevant; Doom is a referendum on the team's stature, not a relational counterparty.

**Failure mode.** Tier-rebuke threats (Doom, Magneto-prime, the Stranger) consistently expose that the Champions are a sub-Avengers, sub-X-Men, sub-FF formation in the Marvel hierarchy. The team has no narrative to absorb this and never develops one.

**Leverage.** Doom does not bother. That is itself the leverage.

---

### Magneto-orbit threats (Sentinels, mutant-targeted operations) — the **identity-axis enemy**

**History.** Late-run Sentinel and mutant-adjacent threats place the Champions inside the X-Men's enemy ecology without granting them the X-Men's narrative weight.

**Dynamics.** See **Sentinels** above; the additional note is that any Magneto-frame engagement positions Warren and Bobby as the *actual* targets and the other three as bodyguards. The team becomes, briefly, a personal security detail for two of its members.

**Failure mode.** Identity-targeted threats fracture the team along mutant/non-mutant lines. There is no shared frame for solidarity.

**Leverage.** Any sustained mutant-rights campaign — by villain or ally — would have pulled Warren and Bobby out and the team would have ended a year earlier.

---

### Griffin, Crimson Dynamo (Dr. Petrovitch in early appearance), Titanium Man, miscellaneous rogues — **filler enemies**

Briefly: these provide the team with mid-tier combat practice and little else. The Griffin engagement is notable for being the rare clean win with low moral cost; Crimson Dynamo / Titanium Man pulls in Romanoff's Soviet history without the team processing it (see **Darkstar** under Lovers/Affiliates, and the broader Romanoff-past silence). Filler enemies are tactically formative and relationally inert.

---

## III. LOVERS, INTIMATES, AND CHARGED PERSONAL BONDS (with the team-level impact mapped)

A team does not have lovers. Its members do, and the love-lives of the founding five exert constant, measurable pressure on the unit. The entries below treat each intimate connection *as it acted on the team*.

### Roxanne Simpson (Blaze's fiancée) — the **off-stage anchor**

**History.** Blaze's pre-Champions intimate, present in his personal life throughout the run, on-panel in the team's orbit intermittently. Roxanne is the human relationship that keeps Blaze coherent enough to function.

**Dynamics.** The team benefits enormously from Roxanne's stabilizing presence without ever crediting it. Blaze is more containable, less prone to off-mission Zarathos episodes, more verbal, when Roxanne is in his life. The team treats this as Blaze's mood; it is, in fact, Roxanne's labor.

**Trust / Safety.** Roxanne is safer than the team for Blaze; the team is unsafer than Roxanne for Blaze.

**Failure mode.** If Roxanne is in distress or distant, Blaze's containment degrades and the team has no protocol. They lose Blaze for stretches and pretend not to notice.

**Leverage.** Anyone wishing to compromise the team via Blaze targets Roxanne. This is well-mapped Ghost Rider lore and the Champions, conspicuously, never set up security for her.

---

### Warren Worthington III ↔ Candy Southern (off-panel for much of the run) — the **adult relationship the team does not have**

**History.** Warren's long-term partner across most of his pre-Apocalypse life. During the Champions run, Candy is intermittently present; she manages aspects of Warren's life that bleed into the team's logistics (residences, social calendar, certain financial conduits).

**Dynamics.** Candy is, functionally, the only *adult* relationship adjacent to the Champions. She models stable partnership in a unit that has none. Her impact on team behavior is indirect but real: Warren is more competent administratively when Candy is engaged, more isolated when she is not.

**Trust / Safety.** High between Warren and Candy; Candy regards the team with a businesswoman's skepticism.

**Failure mode.** Warren over-relies on Candy for emotional regulation he should be doing within the team. The team under-relies on her for the operational skills she would readily provide.

**Leverage.** Warren's emotional stability gates the team's administrative spine. Candy gates Warren's emotional stability. Two degrees of separation; the team never reckons with this.

---

### Bobby Drake's serial dating life — the **avoidance pattern**

**History.** Bobby cycles through romantic interests at a steady, shallow rate across the run.

**Dynamics.** Bobby's pattern is *use the dating life to avoid the identity work*. The team mirrors this — they use the operational life to avoid the identity work, and Bobby's intimacies are a personal-scale version of the group dynamic. The synchrony is invisible to participants.

**Trust / Safety.** Bobby is safe to date in the sense that he is kind; unsafe in the sense that he is not present.

**Failure mode.** Bobby's intimates eventually realize the avoidance and exit; the team's *eventual* dissolution is the macro-version of this micro-pattern.

**Leverage.** A persistent intimate who actually pushed Bobby into identity work would have changed his function in the team; none did during the run.

---

### Hercules's serial conquests — the **performance of vitality**

**History.** Hercules engages women across the run in the manner the comics-era license allowed — frequently, performatively, briefly.

**Dynamics.** Each conquest is, more than a relationship, a *display of Olympian vitality* for the team's benefit. Hercules is shoring up his standing as a figure of mythic potency in a team that elected someone else field leader. The intimacies are real but functionally compensatory.

**Trust / Safety.** Low-investment; rarely consequential.

**Failure mode.** Hercules's performance of vitality competes with the team's performance of teamness for the same scarce resource: belief. When both compete, the team loses, because Hercules's belief in himself is older and louder.

**Leverage.** Hercules's intimacies do not create attack surface; his *bluster* about them creates social-cost surface the team absorbs.

---

### Natasha Romanoff ↔ Matt Murdock (terminated immediately pre-formation) — the **founding wound**

**History.** Natasha arrives at the Champions formation in the wake of


## Powers, Abilities & Their Limits

# POWERS, ABILITIES & THEIR LIMITS — THE CHAMPIONS AS A COMPOSITE WEAPONS SYSTEM

*Subject:* the original Los Angeles Champions as an *aggregate capability*. Individual power-sets are catalogued only insofar as they explain the team's combined envelope — its actual reach, ceiling, failure modes, and the signature that the *shape of the capability mix itself* makes on every engagement they ever ran.

The thesis of this section: the Champions are a **five-vector force package with no integrating doctrine**. Each vector is, in isolation, a competent or extraordinary instrument. Combined, they produce a capability profile that is broad, redundant in the wrong places, blind in the right ones, and impossible to brief on a single page. The team's powers are not the problem; the *interface between them* is.

---

## 1. THE FIVE VECTORS — MECHANISM, RANGE, CEILING

### 1.1 Hercules — Olympian Demigod (Tier: Class-100 / Mythic)

**Mechanism.** Divine physiology, Olympian ichor metabolism, several millennia of accumulated combat experience filed somewhere between procedural memory and mythic narrative. Strength scales reactively to opposition within a wide band; durability is functionally injury-resistant against conventional ordnance and most superhuman strikers; lifespan effectively unbounded barring metaphysical attack.

**Range.** Melee-dominant. Throwing-range extends to the absurd (he hurls cars, statuary, sections of architecture) but he has no native ranged armament beyond improvised mass. Mace of Hercules appears intermittently and is not standardized to team operations.

**Limits.**
- *Magical and metaphysical attacks bypass durability cleanly.* Pluto, the Stranger, Zarathos all operate at frequencies his hide does not register.
- *Cognitive bandwidth narrow under pressure.* He triages by combat instinct, not situational awareness; his peripheral vision for hostage situations, civilian endangerment, environmental cascade is poor.
- *Honor-culture lock-in.* He will not strike from concealment, will not retreat, will not deceive an opponent under arms. This is a *capability limit*, not a stylistic one: there are entire categories of operation he cannot execute even when asked.
- *Chronic intoxication.* See §4. A demigod with a 0.2 BAC is still terrifying; he is also slower, louder, and worse at listening to Romanoff than a sober Hercules would be.

**Growth across the run.** Negligible. He arrives at his ceiling and operates near it; the run does not develop him so much as deploy him.

### 1.2 Black Widow — Peak-Human Operative, Espionage Generalist (Tier: Baseline-Plus)

**Mechanism.** Red Room conditioning — gymnastic and martial-arts training to peak human, accelerated reflex baseline, longevity treatments (canonically extending youth though not yet at Champions-era public knowledge), language facility (Russian, English, French, German fluent; several others working). Equipment: "Widow's Bite" wrist-bracers (electrostatic discharge, variable yield, limited charge), grapple line, sidearm as needed, miscellaneous tradecraft.

**Range.** Effective from intimate (knife-distance) to medium (Bite at 10–15 m, sidearm farther). No standoff capability of consequence.

**Limits.**
- She is the *most fragile body on the team by an order of magnitude* and the *most operationally useful brain*. Every engagement requires the team to protect the asset they most need to keep talking.
- Bite charge is finite and there is no on-panel protocol for resupply during extended engagements.
- Her espionage skill-set (asset development, signals intercept, cover identity, counter-surveillance) is *catastrophically under-utilized* by the team. The Champions never run pre-emptive intelligence. She is deployed as a martial artist with bracers, which is the least valuable use of her.

**Growth across the run.** She *adapts* — accepting field-leader election (#4), beginning to issue tactical orders — but the unit cannot absorb the doctrine she could provide. The growth is hers; it does not propagate.

### 1.3 Ghost Rider — Possession-Vehicle / Spirit of Vengeance (Tier: Mystical, Variable)

**Mechanism.** Johnny Blaze, host; Zarathos (in the era's then-implicit framing, "demon"), passenger. Transformation triggered by darkness, the presence of evil, sometimes emotional extremity, sometimes nothing the team can identify. Hellfire generation (concussive, conflagrant, soul-burning variants), chain-weapon, hellcycle (mystical motorcycle, walls/water/vertical surfaces traversable), Penance Stare (intermittent in this era), enhanced durability of the host body while transformed.

**Range.** Hellfire effective to ~30 m as projectile, area-effect at shorter; chain at melee-plus; cycle gives him battlefield mobility comparable to or exceeding Angel's.

**Limits.**
- *Triggering is involuntary and unstable.* He cannot reliably transform when needed and cannot reliably stay human when needed. This is the team's single most dangerous operational variable.
- *Targeting discrimination by the Zarathos persona is poor.* Hellfire reads moral guilt as legitimate target; in a city of seven million people this produces false positives constantly. The team has no IFF protocol with him.
- *Soul-attack vectors do not respect mission constraints.* Even a "win" with Ghost Rider on the field carries metaphysical collateral.
- *Blaze, untransformed, is a stunt rider and mechanic* — a competent but baseline-human asset who occupies a roster slot.

**Growth across the run.** None integrative. The team never learns to predict or shape his transformation. They learn, instead, to *position him* — typically at edges, peripheries, rear-arc — which is a workaround, not a development.

### 1.4 Iceman — Omega-class Mutant in Pre-Omega Expression (Tier: High, Underdeveloped)

**Mechanism.** Generation and manipulation of ice through ambient moisture conversion; *de facto* cryokinesis and thermal sink. In the Champions era he is operating at the "ice-slide and snowball" expression of what is, in later canon, established as one of the highest-output mutant power signatures in the Marvel field. He does not yet know this. *No one on the team knows this.*

**Range.** Slides extend battlefield mobility to several hundred meters and provide elevated firing platforms; ice projectiles to ~50 m with diminishing accuracy; constructs (walls, shields, bridges) at melee-to-medium.

**Limits.**
- *Self-perception ceiling.* He uses his powers at the output level he believes is normal for them — which is roughly 5–15% of demonstrated later capacity. The largest constraint on Iceman is Iceman's expectation of Iceman.
- *Humid environments amplify, arid environments throttle.* Los Angeles is, on average, the wrong city for him. He compensates with effort he does not narrate.
- *Tactical timidity.* He defaults to non-lethal, defensive, screening applications; offensive use against humanoid targets is reflexively withheld.
- *Comic register as defense.* The jokes are not character color; they are *capability suppression*. Every time he quips mid-fight he is throttling his own escalation by reframing the situation as lower-stakes than it is.

**Growth across the run.** Minimal in raw output, modest in independence. He spends the Champions era in a kind of suspended adolescence of power. The expansion happens *after* — *X-Factor*, *Champions Classic*, *Uncanny* — and is part of why surviving members read the Champions era as wilderness.

### 1.5 Angel — Mutant Flight, Avian Adaptation (Tier: Specialist)

**Mechanism.** Naturally evolved wings (10–16 ft span), hollow-boned skeletal adaptations, enlarged cardiopulmonary capacity, enhanced visual acuity, modest enhanced durability and healing. Pre-Apocalypse, pre-Archangel — no metal wings, no blood-knives, no death-aspect; just the wings and the man.

**Range.** Aerial reconnaissance and transport are his structural contribution; combat application is *strafing*, *grappling*, *intercept*. Carrying capacity ~two persons at degraded performance; cruise speed in the 70–150 mph band depending on conditions; sustained ceiling several thousand feet.

**Limits.**
- *No native offensive armament.* He is a delivery system and an observation platform; he is not a weapon.
- *Vulnerable airframe.* Wings are damageable, slow to heal at full function, and a structural single-point-of-failure for his entire combat identity.
- *Class anxiety as operational constraint.* See §3 — Warren is the *only* member who can fund the team, and the cognitive load of administration suppresses the time he spends developing as a combatant.
- *Identity drift.* He is in the era of greatest uncertainty about whether he is a hero, a businessman, a student, or a celebrity. The wings do not change; the man wearing them keeps swapping out underneath.

**Growth across the run.** He grows as an *organizer* — funding, premises, press, logistics — and stagnates as a *flier*. The team uses him as the wrong instrument.

---

## 2. THE COMPOSITE ENVELOPE — WHAT THE TEAM CAN ACTUALLY DO

Aggregated, the Champions cover a startlingly wide capability matrix:

| Domain | Primary | Backup |
|---|---|---|
| Heavy melee / brute force | Hercules | Ghost Rider |
| Ranged fire | Ghost Rider (hellfire) | Iceman (projectiles), Widow (Bite) |
| Air mobility | Angel | Iceman (slides), Ghost Rider (cycle) |
| Ground mobility | Ghost Rider (cycle) | Iceman (slides) |
| Reconnaissance | Angel (visual), Widow (HUMINT, tradecraft) | — |
| Stealth / infiltration | Widow | — *(single point of failure)* |
| Area denial / battlefield shaping | Iceman (walls, slicks) | Hercules (architectural destruction, accidental) |
| Anti-armor | Hercules | Ghost Rider |
| Anti-mystical | Ghost Rider | — |
| Civilian extraction | Angel | Iceman |
| Medical | — | — *(catastrophic gap)* |
| Communications / coordination | — | — *(no native comms)* |
| Intelligence pre-mission | — | Widow (unused) |

The on-paper coverage is impressive. The off-paper reality is that the team has **two non-redundant single points of failure (Widow for stealth/intel, Angel for aerial reconnaissance), zero medical capacity, zero communications infrastructure, and a power-spread of roughly five orders of magnitude in raw output**. There is no echelon of force between Hercules and Black Widow. There is no soft option below Iceman's constructs. There is no hard option above Hercules.

**Effective deployable envelope:** city-scale conventional super-threats; single-objective engagements with clean moral framing; defensive holding actions where Hercules can anchor and Angel can extract civilians. **Outside the envelope:** counter-intelligence, infiltration, hostage rescue with hard timelines, mystical containment requiring discrimination, anything requiring a graded use-of-force ladder, anything requiring *not being seen*.

---

## 3. INTERFACE FAILURES — THE INTEGRATION DEFICIT

Powers do not fight; *teams* do. The Champions' integration is governed by four pathologies:

**3.1 Scale incommensurability.** Hercules cannot calibrate force against opponents Iceman or Widow are also engaging without risking them. A Hercules-grade impact in a shared melee is a teammate-injury event waiting to happen. Observable across the run: Hercules tends to *peel off* to fight the heaviest opponent solo, which is partly tactical good sense and partly an admission that he cannot share a fight with the others safely.

**3.2 Communications void.** No comms. The team coordinates by *line-of-sight and shouting*. Angel's aerial position, which should be a force multiplier as an overwatch and spotter, is degraded to a delivery role because he cannot reliably relay to the ground in real time. Widow's tradecraft — which assumes a comms architecture — runs at a fraction of effectiveness.

**3.3 No common ROE.** Hercules will not strike from behind; Widow will if the mission requires; Ghost Rider strikes by moral signature he alone perceives; Iceman withholds; Angel grapples. Five rules of engagement, never reconciled. Every fight is a *negotiation in real time* about what is permissible, which the team paves over with combat tempo.

**3.4 No after-action review.** The team never debriefs. Capability lessons (Rampage's escalation, Darkstar's onboarding, Sentinels' adaptation) are not converted into doctrine because no doctrinal forum exists. Each engagement starts from approximately the same baseline as the last one.

---

## 4. SIGNATURE APPLICATIONS — HOW THE TEAM ACTUALLY FIGHTS

Despite the integration deficit, repeated combats produce identifiable signatures:

- **The "Hercules anchor" formation.** Hercules engages the apex threat in a fixed location; the others orbit, drawing off support and protecting non-combatants. Works against single-apex threats (Typhon, Pluto's lieutenants). Fails against distributed threats (Sentinels, Swarm) where there is no apex to anchor against.
- **The Angel-Iceman pairing.** Bobby slides, Warren flies; together they cover the aerial and elevated dimensions. Their X-Men residual chemistry produces the team's most genuinely *integrated* sub-unit. They are functionally a two-man team inside the five-man team, which is a sign of the larger team's failure to integrate them outward.
- **The Widow-as-tip-of-spear sequence.** When she leads (post-#4), the operation acquires shape — staging, sequencing, fallback. When she does not, it does not. Her field leadership is the only on-panel evidence of doctrine in the run.
- **The Ghost Rider as terminal argument.** When negotiation, deception, and conventional force have failed, Blaze transforms and the engagement ends — usually messily, sometimes catastrophically for the antagonist's soul. The team uses him as a closer, which means they have *implicitly* agreed to a moral premise (that the opponent deserves what Zarathos does) that they have never *explicitly* debated.
- **The improvised civilian extraction.** Angel carries, Iceman slides, Hercules clears debris, Widow secures perimeter, Ghost Rider — held back. This is the formation in which the "for the common man" mandate actually shows. It is also the formation least often required by the threats they draw.

---

## 5. GROWTH CURVES — WHO DEVELOPS, WHO DOES NOT, AND WHY

| Member | Raw-power growth in Champions era | Skill growth | Doctrinal contribution |
|---|---|---|---|
| Hercules | None (ceiling) | None | Honor-culture template (rigid) |
| Black Widow | None (ceiling) | Some — accepts leadership | The only doctrine present |
| Ghost Rider | None | None (control not developed) | None |
| Iceman | Trace | Trace | None |
| Angel | None | Considerable — as an administrator | Mission statement only |

**The pattern:** the two members with ceiling room (Iceman, Ghost Rider) do not grow; the two members at ceiling (Hercules, Widow) cannot grow; the one member who grows (Angel) grows in a direction *away from* combat capability. The team's aggregate combat potential is *flat to declining* across its run, even as its members individually retain or build capability that flowers in subsequent books. **The Champions specifically is a setting in which these people do not get better.**

This is consistent with the §3 (Psychological) read: a group that substitutes operational tempo for processing cannot extract learning from its own experience.

---

## 6. SYMPTOM-AS-SIGNATURE — WHAT THE POWER-MIX REVEALS

Read the roster as a single symptom and the diagnosis writes itself.

**A god, a spy, a damned man, a boy made of ice, a man with wings.** This is not a team composition; it is a *list of refusals to be ordinary*. Each member's power is also each member's *exile*: Hercules from mortality, Widow from innocence, Blaze from his own body, Bobby from puberty, Warren from the ground. The Champions are a coalition of people who could not stay in the category they were born into. The "for the common man" mandate is poignant precisely because **no member of the team is, or can be, a common man**. The roster *forecloses* the mission at the level of physiology.

**The power-spread is a portrait of the attachment style.** Five orders of magnitude of raw output, no shared scale, no shared metabolism, no shared mortality — the somatic facts mirror the relational facts. A team whose bodies cannot share a stretcher cannot share a self.

**The integration deficit is the team's biography.** No comms, no ROE, no doctrine, no debrief. These are not omissions; they are the *substantive content* of "we got together too fast." A team that had courted before marrying would have these. The Champions eloped.

**The growth curves are the prognosis.** Members who develop elsewhere do not develop here. The Champions is not a holding environment in which capability matures; it is a holding pattern in which capability waits. Every survivor's later flourishing — Iceman's omega expression, Widow's Avengers tenure, Warren's transformations, Blaze's later integrations, Hercules's continued epic — happens *after the exit*. The team's signature, finally, is **arrested development under the appearance of activity**: an enormous quantity of fighting that does not, in the end, change anyone who did it.

That is what their powers, taken together, *say*. The file notes it and moves on.

— UATU, observing.


## Operational Profile & Tradecraft

# OPERATIONAL PROFILE & TRADECRAFT — THE CHAMPIONS (Los Angeles, Original Roster)

*Filed as Section 6 of the working dossier. Cross-reference Sections 1 (History), 3 (Psychological), and 4 (Psychiatric); this section operationalizes their findings into the question a surveillance file actually has to answer: how, mechanically, does this unit conduct its life?*

---

## 6.1 Decision-Making Architecture

**Formal structure (on paper).** Field leadership: Black Widow, by election of the membership (*Champions* #4). Executive/financial authority: Warren Worthington III, by sole capitalization. Public-facing spokesperson: rotates by availability, weighted toward Hercules (who enjoys it) and Iceman (who is the most photogenic in civilian clothes). Chair, board of Champions of Los Angeles, Inc.: Worthington. Corporate secretary, *de facto*: Worthington's personal counsel and the Worthington family attorneys, imported wholesale.

**Actual structure (observed).** A **dual-axis duopoly** with a vetoing minority of one. Romanoff calls the tactical shot in the moment; Hercules ratifies, modifies, or — in roughly one engagement in four — simply overrides by acting before consultation completes. Worthington holds a silent veto via the checkbook he never explicitly threatens to close. Blaze, Drake, and (when present) Darkstar are functionally enlisted personnel: they are *consulted* on missions and *informed* on everything else. There is no voting record after the founding leadership election. There are no minutes. There is no agenda.

**Decision latency.** Under threat: sub-second, executed via line-of-sight tactical hand signals Romanoff has imported from her tradecraft and progressively taught the rest. At rest: catastrophic. Major non-combat decisions (hiring Darkstar onto the roster, accepting press, responding to civic authorities) are typically made within a single conversation in whatever room the relevant members happen to be standing in, and never revisited. There is no standing meeting. There is no review cycle.

**Information flow.** Asymmetric and lossy. Romanoff knows operationally relevant information about each teammate's prior life that she does not share. Worthington knows financial and legal exposure data he does not share. Hercules knows mythological-tier threat intelligence he does not share *because it does not occur to him that mortals would need to be briefed*. Blaze knows the metaphysical specifications of his own condition and shares them only when episode-forced. Drake knows nothing he is not told and tells everyone everything he knows. The net is a team in which the two most powerful information nodes (Romanoff, Worthington) operate compartmentalized, and the most powerful raw-output node (Hercules) operates uninformed.

**Decision pathology.** Per Section 3: action substitutes for deliberation. The decision rule that best fits observed behavior is "*if any member proposes a kinetic response, the team executes it unless Romanoff actively countermands*." Romanoff countermands roughly once per arc. The team thus operates, in practice, on a **Hercules-default with Romanoff-interrupt** logic — a single point of failure if Romanoff is incapacitated, absent, or (as in the Darkstar onboarding) choosing not to exercise her judgment for reasons of her own.

---

## 6.2 Methods and Tradecraft

**Engagement doctrine.** None, formally. Emergent doctrine, reverse-engineered from seventeen issues of behavior: **engage on contact, sort objectives in flight, extract on victory, debrief never.** The Champions do not stake out, do not surveil, do not infiltrate. With the singular exception of Romanoff's solo excursions (which she treats as personal sidebars, not team operations), every recorded engagement is reactive — initiated by the adversary, by a 911-equivalent civilian call routed through the LA premises, or by Worthington's PR office fielding a press inquiry the team then converts into a deployment.

**Tactical signature.** A **diamond-with-air-cap formation** emerges by mid-run: Hercules as point/anvil, Iceman and Black Widow as left and right flanks providing area-denial and precision respectively, Ghost Rider as mobile heavy at the rear or roaming, Angel as overwatch/extraction at altitude. This formation is *never named or drawn up* in any panel; it is muscle memory accreted from repetition. They fall into it within two seconds of contact. When the formation is denied (indoor environments, anti-aerial enemies, Angel grounded), the team's effectiveness drops measurably; they have no second formation.

**Romanoff's contribution to team tradecraft.** Considerable but under-deployed. She introduces, over the run: hand-signal vocabulary (initially Soviet-derived, progressively Americanized); a primitive comms protocol using earpieces wired through the headquarters switchboard; basic counter-surveillance sweeps of the Champions' own premises (which she performs alone, without telling the team, and which she finds positive results on more than once); and a one-page operational brief she drafts before missions she has time to plan. The team treats the briefs as a Natasha eccentricity and frequently fails to read them. She stops drafting them by issue ten.

**Worthington's contribution to operational infrastructure.** Almost everything that is not combat: the lease, the renovation, the vehicles, the comms switchboard, the press apparatus, the legal shield, the payroll for support staff. He performs this labor in a register of *cheerful invisibility* — he does not bill the team for it, does not reference it in meetings, and would find it gauche to do so. The result: the team consistently *underestimates the operational fragility their entire existence rests on*, because the member providing the infrastructure refuses to make it salient.

**Hercules's contribution to tradecraft.** Negative. He is a one-being shock-and-awe package whose presence on the field collapses the need for tradecraft and therefore *prevents the team from developing it*. He does not patrol. He does not scout. He does not maintain comms discipline (he announces himself, by name, in the third person, to enemies who are trying to ambush him). He treats stealth as a discourtesy.

**Blaze/Ghost Rider's contribution.** Asymmetric. As Blaze: skilled motorcyclist, competent at vehicle pursuit, useful at civilian-facing crowd work (he reads as Hollywood blue-collar and people talk to him). As Ghost Rider: a metaphysical artillery piece with no friend-or-foe discrimination protocol. The team's operational accommodation is to *position him where collateral does not matter* — which in Los Angeles is rarely available, and which the team negotiates ad hoc, often badly.

**Drake's contribution.** Versatile, under-utilized. Ice constructs as cover, as bridges, as restraints, as projectile, as area-denial. The team uses him almost exclusively for the last two; the constructional and engineering applications of his power are barely explored. He is the team's most *tactically flexible* member and is read by the others as its most junior, which is a misreading they never correct.

**Counter-intelligence posture.** Theoretical: Romanoff-grade. Actual: civilian-grade with Romanoff personally compensating in the background. The headquarters has been bugged at least twice in-run by adversaries; Romanoff finds and removes the devices without team meeting. Personnel security is nil — Darkstar's onboarding (Section 1) is the load-bearing example: a defector from a foreign service is admitted to operational membership inside a single issue, with no isolation period, no debrief, no clearance review. *Romanoff, the one member who knows exactly what such a clearance would look like, does not insist on one.* The most parsimonious read is that she has privately concluded the team is not worth securing — a damning operational judgment from the member with the most professional standing to make it.

**OPSEC tells.**
- Public address by superhero name in civilian venues.
- Civilian identities of Worthington, Drake, and Blaze are essentially open secrets in Los Angeles by issue ten.
- Headquarters location publicly known; no hardened perimeter beyond what Worthington's contractors built into the original lease.
- Vehicles registered to recognizable shells of Worthington holdings — penetrable by any competent investigator inside an afternoon.
- Press releases issued in the team's name without legal review on at least two occasions; both produced exposure Worthington's attorneys then quietly absorbed.

---

## 6.3 Resources and Finances

**Capital structure.** Single-source equity. The Champions of Los Angeles is, in everything but the chart, a Worthington family office sub-vehicle. Funding flow: Worthington personal trust → operating account → premises, payroll, equipment, insurance, legal, PR. No external donors. No municipal contracts. No federal grants (the team's anti-establishment posture would in any event preclude these). No merchandising despite the photogenic surface — Worthington personally vetoes licensing on the grounds it would cheapen the mission, which is itself diagnostic of his unilateral cultural authority.

**Burn rate (inferred).** A West Coast headquarters with the staffing and PR profile observable in the run is not a six-figure operation; it is low seven-figure annual, in mid-1970s dollars, scaling with combat-incident liability. Worthington can absorb this indefinitely from trust income without touching principal. He does not appear to track it closely. *Nobody else on the team has any idea what it costs to be a Champion.*

**Insurance and liability.** Carried, at considerable cost, through Worthington's family broker. Property damage from combat is largely settled through a Worthington-funded claims process the public never sees a paperwork edge of. The team's reputation for "not leaving citizens with the bill" is **purchased**, not earned through restraint — they cause as much collateral damage per engagement as any super-team, and the bill is silently picked up.

**Equipment.**
- Romanoff: personal kit (Widow's Line, Bites, costume, sidearms when situationally indicated) maintained out of her own funds and from quartermaster channels she retains from prior service. She does not requisition through Worthington and would resent being asked to.
- Iceman, Angel, Hercules: organic. No equipment line.
- Ghost Rider: motorcycle, mystically self-maintaining; civilian wardrobe Worthington's housekeeper launders.
- Team comms, vehicles, premises security, medical bay (such as it is), training space: all Worthington capex.

**Real property.** The Los Angeles headquarters (interior renders, *Champions* #4 onward): a converted commercial structure on the Westside, modified for hangar-style ingress, a press-facing lobby, training floor, individual quarters for live-in members (Drake and Hercules use these regularly; Blaze sporadically; Romanoff almost never; Worthington has his own residence and commutes). Held in a Worthington-controlled LLC; never on the team's balance sheet because the team has no balance sheet meaningfully distinct from Worthington's.

**Personnel.** Support staff: receptionist/switchboard operator (daytime), part-time press liaison from Worthington's PR firm, retained legal counsel on call, contracted facilities and security personnel for the premises. *No medical staff.* *No chaplain or counselor.* *No quartermaster.* *No archivist or records officer.* The administrative thinness of the operation is striking for its public profile.

**Cash handling and the Romanoff exception.** Romanoff maintains independent operating funds the team is not briefed on, accessed through aliases preserved from prior service. She uses these for travel and asset-running she conducts under Champions cover without informing the team. This is a structural problem the team does not know it has: their field leader is also a freelance operator using their letterhead.

---

## 6.4 Infrastructure and Tempo

**Premises.** As above. Notable interior tells: a common room with a television tuned to news (kept on during waking hours, the team's primary intake of mission-triggering information — *they learn about most of their cases from the eleven o'clock news, which is not an intelligence apparatus, it is the absence of one*); a kitchen Hercules dominates and overstocks; a roof access Angel uses for ingress/egress; a basement vehicle bay; a training room used most by Romanoff (alone, early morning) and Drake (intermittently, with music).

**Operational tempo.** Episodic and externally driven. There is no patrol schedule. The team does not "go out looking" for anything. Days pass without operational activity; then an incident hits, the team converges (response time from full-roster scatter is approximately twelve to twenty minutes inside LA County, dictated by Angel's flight time and Romanoff's drive from wherever she actually sleeps), engages, resolves, returns, scatters again.

**Inter-mission behavior.** Each member retreats to a personal pattern: Hercules to wine and the kitchen and occasional public appearances he enjoys for the attention; Romanoff out of the premises entirely, to whereabouts she does not disclose; Worthington to family business and the philanthropic circuit; Drake to college coursework he is intermittently still pretending to pursue and to dating; Blaze to Roxanne when she will see him and to solitary rides when she will not. The premises sit largely empty between calls. *A team that does not live together cannot become one.*

**Communications.** Switchboard-routed earpieces (Romanoff's contribution) for active missions; landline to the premises for civilian-facing intake; press releases through the Worthington PR firm; no internal classified channel; no encrypted traffic; no dead-drop or fallback protocol. If the switchboard is taken out, the team has no comms.

**Records and institutional memory.** Effectively nil. No mission log is maintained. After-action reports do not exist. The team's institutional knowledge lives entirely in the memories of its members and dies with each member's exit. This is one mechanism by which the post-dissolution amnesia documented in Section 1 becomes possible: *there is no archive to remember from.*

---

## 6.5 Patterns of Action

**The intake-to-engagement pipeline.** Threat surfaces (via news broadcast, civilian call to the switchboard, Worthington's society/business network occasionally flagging a corporate-tier incident, or direct attack on the premises). Whoever is on-site decides the team responds. Switchboard pages the off-site members. Convergence at the scene, not the premises — the team almost never assembles before deploying; they meet *at the fight*. Engagement proceeds on the diamond-with-air-cap reflex (6.2). Resolution is declared when the adversary is incapacitated or retreats; civilian authorities arrive; the team departs *before* taking questions, almost without exception. Press handling occurs afterward, through the PR firm.

**Conflict resolution within the team.** Argued in the open, in the moment, almost never resolved. The Hercules/Romanoff command friction recurs at a cadence of roughly once every two or three issues and is *the same argument every time*: Hercules acts unilaterally; Romanoff confronts him in front of the others; Hercules apologizes in courtly register; nothing structural changes; repeat. The team has no mechanism for converting a recurring conflict into a policy.

**Onboarding and offboarding.** Onboarding: as documented (Darkstar), effectively absent. Offboarding: also absent. When members drift (Romanoff's increasing absences in the back half of the run; Blaze's lengthening Zarathos episodes off-panel), no acknowledgment is made, no replacement is sought, no exit interview occurs. The team treats roster fluctuation as weather.

**Relations with peer institutions.**
- *Avengers:* studiously avoided. The Champions decline to coordinate even on geographically logical handoffs; this is mission-statement-driven and Warren-articulated, and the other members ratify by silence.
- *X-Men adjacency:* personal, through Drake and Worthington; not operational. The Champions do not invoke X-Men resources or share intelligence with them, even when mutant-tier threats (Sentinels, Magneto-adjacent) clearly warrant it. This is *operationally indefensible* and reads as Worthington and Drake's reluctance to surface the Champions to Xavier's judgment.
- *LAPD and civic authorities:* cordial-distant. The team complies with crime-scene perimeter requests, departs before statements, and absorbs property damage privately so as not to give civic authorities a budgetary lever over them.
- *S.H.I.E.L.D.:* Romanoff handles, alone, off-channel. The team is not informed of these contacts.
- *Press:* managed through Worthington's PR apparatus. Members are coached not to grant unscheduled interviews; they grant unscheduled interviews routinely (Hercules and Drake the worst offenders; Romanoff never).

**Use of force.** Escalation is not protocolized; it is *temperament-distributed*. Romanoff applies graduated force with discrimination. Drake applies non-lethal force by default and is reluctant to escalate. Worthington is similarly restrained, with the additional limit of his power-set's lower ceiling. Blaze applies whatever Zarathos applies, which is uncalibrated and on at least one occasion ethically catastrophic. Hercules applies Olympian force at all settings, treating mortal-tier opponents and Class-100 threats with comparable enthusiasm. The team has no rules of engagement document. Each fight ends at the force level set by whichever member commits first.

---

## 6.6 Signature Operational Tells

These are the granular fingerprints — the kind of detail a surveillance product collates over months — that *uniquely identify this unit in motion*:

- **The news-as-tasking tell.** A team whose operations correlate, with high statistical fidelity, to the LA evening broadcast lineup. Pull a week of the team's deployments against the local news cycle: the overlap is nearly one-to-one. They are reactive to the same stimulus a civilian audience sees.
- **The Worthington-arrives-last tell.** Worthington flies under his own power and is faster point-to-point than every teammate except Ghost Rider on open road; nevertheless he is consistently the last to the scene by thirty to ninety seconds, because he stops to put on the costume he refuses to wear under civilian clothes. A trained observer can use his arrival to time the team's full assembly.
- **The Hercules announcement tell.** Engagements involving Hercules are preceded by an audible third-person self-identification ("Now shall the son of Zeus —"). This is so consistent that adversaries with even basic intelligence could use it as a one-second early-warning trigger; none observably do, which says more about the adversaries than the tell.
- **The Romanoff pre-sweep.** On unfamiliar premises, Romanoff conducts a perceptible perimeter walk before the team settles. It looks like restlessness; it is reconnaissance. She does it in civilian environments too — restaurants, press venues, allied facilities — and does not stop doing it for anyone's comfort.
- **The Drake-jokes-when-frightened tell.** Iceman's verbal output rate spikes inversely with team morale. A surveillance officer tracking the team's internal stress state can read it off Drake's quip cadence with high reliability.
- **The Blaze-disappears tell.** In the hours before a Zarathos episode, Blaze becomes noticeably withdrawn and physically distances from the others — pacing, smoking outdoors, leaving the premises on the bike without stated destination. The team has learned to notice this and has not learned to act on it.
- **The empty-headquarters tell.** Premises lights and activity profile, surveilled across a week, would show occupancy patterns no genuine residential or operational facility produces: empty days, then full-roster convergence inside twenty minutes, then re-empty. The building *performs* a team that does not live in it.
- **The "press leaves before the cleanup arrives" tell.** Photographers consistently get post-engagement shots that include Worthington's PR liaison but not the contractors who arrive to handle property damage. The handoff is choreographed. The PR firm has, demonstrably, a standing arrangement to clear press before claims adjusters appear.
- **The wine-bottle inventory.** Hercules's consumption is logged, accidentally, by the kitchen's recycling output — a metric a janitorial-access source could quantify. The volume would, in a non-Olympian, prompt clinical intervention.
- **The unread brief.** A folded one-pager on the common-room table, in Romanoff's hand, on mission days during the first half of the run. After issue ten, its absence is itself a tell — the field leader has stopped trying to brief the team, and any observer with prior baseline can date the team's interior collapse from the day the briefs stop appearing.

---

## 6.7 Net Operational Assessment

The Champions of Los Angeles operate as a **reactive, single-sourced, infrastructurally fragile, doctrinally improvisational super-team with one professional intelligence officer who has tacitly conceded the unit is not worth professionalizing, one financial patron whose invisibility of effort masks the team's total dependence on him, one demigod whose presence preempts the development of the tradecraft the team needs, and three members who fill out the roster without shaping the operational character.** Their tactical chemistry under contact is genuinely impressive and would be the envy of better-formed teams. Everything between contacts — intake, planning, intelligence, security, records, succession, governance — ranges from vestigial to absent.

A surveillance product on this unit writes itself in a fortnight. A penetration of this unit, by any adversary willing to invest a Darkstar-pattern legend, succeeds inside a month. The Champions are observable, predictable, and operationally porous. That they accomplished what they did, for as long as they did, is a testament to the individual quality of the members and to the absorptive capacity of Worthington's checkbook — not to anything resembling tradecraft.

— UATU, observing.


## Worldview & Operational Code

# Worldview & Operational Code — The Champions (Los Angeles, Original Roster)

*A belief system reconstructed not from a manifesto — the team never wrote one — but from what they consistently did, refused to do, said in press conferences they immediately contradicted, and assumed without ever stating. The Champions held a coherent worldview; they simply never noticed they held it.*

---

## 1. THE ROOT AXIOM — "PROXIMITY IS LEGITIMACY"

The deepest belief in the Champions' operational code, the one from which nearly all the others descend, is the conviction that **a hero's right to act is conferred by physical and emotional nearness to the public**, not by sanction, mandate, election, or institutional charter. The Avengers are right because they fight in New York where the press lives; the Champions believe they will be right because they fight in Los Angeles where ordinary people *live*. The premise is never argued; it is treated as self-evident from the moment Warren articulates the West Coast mission.

This axiom produces several immediate corollaries the team obeys without examining:

- **Storefronts over headquarters.** The team prefers a publicly-accessible Los Angeles office to a fortified base. The choice is read internally as humility; it is also, unexamined, a security catastrophe Romanoff would never have tolerated in any other professional context.
- **Walk-in clients over briefings.** The team accepts cases largely as they arrive at the door, by phone, by news broadcast. There is no triage layer, no intake protocol, no case-selection criterion beyond "is someone in front of us asking."
- **Press as constituency.** The team conflates the journalists who cover them with the "common man" they exist to serve. Public favorability is treated as a proxy for mission success — a category error the team never catches.

The axiom is sincere. It is also the precise mechanism by which the Champions confuse *being seen* with *being needed*.

---

## 2. THE THEORY OF CONFLICT — "FIGHTS ARE CONVERSATIONS THE WORLD STARTED"

The Champions do not, as an organism, believe in prevention. They believe in **response**. Conflict is conceptualized as something that *happens to them*, to which they then bring force and virtue. The team has, across seventeen issues, no instance of pre-emptive intelligence work, no surveillance program, no analyst function, no scenario-planning exercise. Romanoff — who in any other deployment would build exactly these capacities — does not build them here. The reason is doctrinal, not lazy: the team's *self-concept as moral actors depends on being the answer rather than the question.*

This produces a specific theory of violence:

- **Violence is licit when arriving.** When attacked, the team escalates fluently and without guilt. The Pluto/Ares assault that births them is repelled at god-tier force levels with no after-action reflection.
- **Violence is morally fraught when initiated.** The team is markedly uncomfortable with raids, ambushes, or first-strike postures. When Romanoff suggests them, she is gently overridden, usually by Hercules's honor-frame ("we do not strike from shadow") or Warren's optics-frame ("how will it look").
- **Violence against the symbolically-aligned is destabilizing.** Rampage — a wronged businessman in armor — fractures the team's moral consensus precisely because punching him *feels like* punching the constituency. The team has no doctrine for adversaries who occupy the moral position the team claims to defend.

Conflict, in the Champions' code, is therefore a kind of grammar in which the team's role is to be the predicate, never the subject. They will *meet* a sentence the world starts; they will not *begin* one.

---

## 3. THE THEORY OF CONTROL — "CHAINS OF COMMAND ARE FOR PEOPLE WHO DO NOT TRUST EACH OTHER"

The Champions hold, implicitly and disastrously, that **explicit hierarchy is a confession of relational weakness**. The election of Romanoff as field leader (#4) is treated by the team as a paperwork formality — the kind of thing one does because someone has to be it — rather than as a load-bearing authority assignment. Hercules's refusal to defer cleanly is not, in his frame, insubordination; it is the natural conduct of an immortal among mortals who has graciously consented to *participate in* a structure rather than *submit to* one.

The unexamined belief structure here:

- **Authority is granted by being, not by role.** Hercules commands because he is a god; Romanoff leads because she is competent; Warren funds because he is rich; Blaze acts because he is haunted; Bobby executes because he is asked. Each operates from a different theory of why they get to do what they do, and none of the theories is interoperable with the others.
- **Decisions should emerge.** The team prefers consensus by attrition — talk until someone with enough force-of-personality (almost always Hercules) ends the conversation — to formal decision protocols. This is sincerely believed to be more democratic; it is in fact more oligarchic, since it transfers decision rights to whoever has the loudest baseline.
- **Rules are insults.** Standing operating procedures, rules of engagement, vetting protocols, intake screening — the entire apparatus of professionalized teamwork — are felt by the team as accusations of distrust against each other. Building them would, in the team's emotional logic, *concede* that the unit might not work.

The result is an operation that runs on charisma, mood, and improvisation, and that interprets every attempt to formalize as a betrayal of the founding warmth. This is the worldview of a band, not a unit.

---

## 4. THE THEORY OF TRUST — "TRUST IS WHAT YOU DEMONSTRATE UNDER FIRE, NOTHING ELSE COUNTS"

The Champions operate a **somatic theory of trust**: trustworthiness is established by taking damage for a teammate, by the body in motion next to another body in motion, by the shared inhalation before a charge. Trust is *not* established by biographical disclosure, background vetting, ideological alignment, or time elapsed.

This is why Darkstar — a KGB-aligned defector — is integrated in essentially one issue with no security review. By the team's lights, she has fought beside them; she has therefore been trusted. The category "things one might still need to know about her" does not exist in the Champions' epistemology, because the only knowledge that counts has already been generated.

The corollaries:

- **Off-duty intimacy is not pursued.** Members do not interrogate each other about pasts, hometowns, fears, prior relationships. The information is not felt to be load-bearing; the unit has trust without it. This is true and also catastrophic — Romanoff's Red Room conditioning, Blaze's Zarathos status, Hercules's Olympian estrangements, Warren's identity drift, Bobby's unresolved adolescence are all unmetabolized at the group level.
- **Betrayal is unimaginable in advance and devastating in actuality.** Because trust is established by combat and combat alone, the team has no framework for a teammate who is loyal in fights but compromised in other registers (psychological, financial, supernatural). Any such compromise, if surfaced, would not be processable.
- **Strangers earn trust faster than they should and old allies do not get re-vetted.** The intake door is wide; the exit door is closed. Once in, always in.

This theory of trust is what produces the team's signature emotional texture: real warmth under fire, real distance between fires, and a strange inability to grow closer over time, because the variable that would deepen closeness — biographical knowledge — is not in the system's accepted inputs.

---

## 5. THE THEORY OF RISK — "RISK IS BORNE BY THE STRONGEST BODY PRESENT"

The Champions' risk doctrine is unspoken and physiological: **whoever can take the hit, takes the hit**. Hercules is therefore positioned at the front of nearly every engagement, not by tactical design but by tacit deference to power-curve. This is read by the team as efficiency; it is in fact a moral abdication, because it means the team never has to negotiate the harder question of *which risks are worth taking at all*.

The unexamined beliefs underneath:

- **Risk is a property of bodies, not of missions.** The team does not ask "is this mission worth its expected cost"; it asks "who among us can survive the expected cost." This collapses the strategic question into the casting question.
- **Mortal risk to non-Hercules members is treated as exceptional and intolerable.** When Romanoff, Bobby, or Warren is endangered, the response is acute and personal. When Hercules is endangered, the response is procedural — *he can take it*. This is correct biologically and corrosive morally; it teaches the team that one member's pain is free.
- **Collateral risk to civilians is calculated by Warren's optics-frame, not by an ethics-frame.** "How will this look" substitutes for "how will this harm." The two answers usually coincide, which lets the team avoid noticing that it is not actually running the second calculation.
- **Reputational risk is borne collectively but managed individually.** Each member, when criticized in press, defends themselves first and the team second. There is no unified communications doctrine, because there is no unified self to communicate from.

The risk doctrine produces a team that is brave in the wrong granularity — willing to bleed for each other, unwilling to deliberate about *whether the bleeding is purchasing anything*.

---

## 6. THE THEORY OF AUTHORITY AND INSTITUTIONS — "WE ARE THE ANSWER TO THE QUESTION OTHER TEAMS BECAME"

The Champions hold a structured allergy to institutional power that is unusual in its specificity. They are not anti-government in the libertarian sense, not anti-Avengers in the rivalrous sense; they are **allergic to becoming what those institutions became**. The underlying belief is that *establishment*, as a process, is corrosive — that any team that accepts charter, oversight, liaison, or budget becomes the thing it was meant to oppose. The Avengers, in the Champions' implicit reading, started as the Champions and ended as a club.

This produces:

- **Refusal of charter.** No SHIELD liaison, no Avengers reciprocity agreement, no municipal partnership with LAPD. The team operates in a deliberate jurisdictional vacuum.
- **Suspicion of credentialing.** Press passes are accepted; security clearances are not pursued. The team would rather be loved than recognized.
- **Self-narration as alternative.** The Champions describe themselves, when asked, almost entirely in negative terms — what they are *not* (not Avengers, not government, not exclusive, not elite). The positive content is thin. This is the rhetoric of a movement, deployed by a small business.
- **Inability to escalate to peers.** When the Sentinels or Doom-tier threats arrive, the team does not call the Avengers, does not call the X-Men, does not call SHIELD. The phone call would feel like surrender. They fight above their weight class out of doctrine, not capability assessment.

The institutional allergy is genuine and, in places, prescient — the Champions are correctly diagnosing a real ossification in their peer teams. They are also using the diagnosis as a license not to build the institutional muscles that would let them outlast their founding warmth.

---

## 7. THE THEORY OF SELF — "WE WILL FIGURE OUT WHO WE ARE BY DOING IT"

The Champions believe, without ever stating it, that **identity is generated by activity rather than by reflection**. They will become the Champions by *being* the Champions — by taking cases, by appearing in headlines, by repelling threats. The work will retroactively define the worker. This is a sincerely-held metaphysics of selfhood, and it is wrong for groups in a way it can sometimes be right for individuals.

The corollaries:

- **No founding retreat, no charter session, no values exercise.** The activities that other teams use to constitute themselves are skipped not by oversight but by conviction. To do them would be to admit the self was not already there.
- **Mid-action improvisation as identity formation.** The team's ethical commitments are negotiated in the middle of fights, on a case-by-case basis, and never codified. Each negotiation is treated as a one-off; the cumulative pattern is never extracted into principle.
- **The name does the work the discussion did not.** "The Champions" is meant to mean something self-evident — a champion is for someone, of something, against an adversary. The team treats the noun as having already done the philosophical labor.
- **Disagreement is read as immaturity, not as data.** When members differ on first principles (Hercules's honor code, Romanoff's pragmatism, Blaze's guilt, Warren's noblesse, Bobby's intuitionism), the disagreement is shelved rather than excavated, because excavation would require admitting the self has not yet been generated.

The self-theory produces a team that is permanently *about to know* who it is. The knowledge never arrives because the conditions for its arrival — sustained reflection, formal codification, willingness to discover that members disagree fundamentally — are forbidden by the theory itself.

---

## 8. THE THEORY OF THE PUBLIC — "THE COMMON MAN WILL RECOGNIZE US AS THEIRS"

The Champions hold a romantic, undefended belief in **automatic public recognition**. The mandate "for the common man" assumes the common man will, on encountering the team, immediately experience the team as his. This belief is never tested before it is operationalized, and the test cases (Rampage prototypically, but also the general LA citizenry) repeatedly fail to confirm it.

The underlying beliefs:

- **The public is a unified addressee.** The team speaks to "the common man" as if such a person existed in the singular and shared a single set of needs. The actual Los Angeles public — multiracial, multilingual, economically stratified, ideologically heterogeneous — is never engaged in its actual plurality. The team has no Spanish-speaking liaison, no neighborhood-specific outreach, no class-conscious case selection.
- **Service is performed rather than negotiated.** The Champions decide what the public needs (protection from large-scale threats) and supply it; they do not ask. There are no town halls, no community boards, no feedback channels. The "for the common man" relation is one-way.
- **Hostility from the served is read as misunderstanding.** When Rampage returns radicalized, when civilians criticize, when the press turns, the team's first interpretation is always *they have not understood us yet*. The interpretation *we have not understood them* is structurally unavailable.
- **Aesthetics substitute for accountability.** Being visible, being photogenic, being available for interviews is treated as the discharge of public obligation. Audit, transparency reports, civilian oversight — none of these are pursued or, apparently, conceived.

This theory of the public is the team's most generous and most paternalistic belief. It is generous because it sincerely intends service. It is paternalistic because it never imagines the served might have opinions about the service. The "common man" of the Champions' code is a noble abstraction; the actual public of Los Angeles is a constituency the team never quite meets.

---

## 9. THE THEORY OF SUFFERING — "PAIN IS PRIVATE, MISSION IS SHARED"

Each member arrives at the team carrying acute attachment loss or trauma. None of these are processed in the group setting. The Champions hold, implicitly, that **suffering is the property of the sufferer, and the team's role is to provide activity sufficient to displace it**. This is not callousness; it is a sincere theory of care, in which respecting a member's pain means not making them talk about it.

The corollaries:

- **Mission as analgesia.** Members in distress are offered work, not conversation. Romanoff post-Murdock, Blaze post-Zarathos episode, Hercules post-Olympus, Warren post-X-Men — all are absorbed into operations rather than into dialogue.
- **No clinician, no chaplain, no debrief.** The team has no role for emotional metabolism. The omission is doctrinal: introducing such a role would be to admit that combat and camaraderie are insufficient, which the worldview cannot concede.
- **Substance use is privatized.** Hercules's drinking is treated as his to manage. Romanoff's history of pharmacological conditioning is treated as hers to manage. The team does not intervene because intervening would breach the rule that pain is private.
- **Grief is skipped.** When losses occur (allied casualties, missed civilians, failures), the team does not mourn together. They move to the next case. The grief accrues somewhere — visible in the post-dissolution embarrassment of survivors — but never in the room.

The suffering doctrine produces a team that is kind in intention and isolating in effect. Every member is left to carry their own weight, while the group performs solidarity in combat that does not translate to solidarity in pain.

---

## 10. THE THEORY OF TIME — "WE ARE FOREVER UNTIL WE ARE NOT"

The Champions have no longitudinal self-conception. There is no five-year plan, no succession plan, no sunset clause, no theory of how the team would know to disband. The implicit belief is that **the team exists in a perpetual present** — that the question of duration is either uninteresting or premature.

This produces:

- **No succession.** There is no protocol for adding members beyond ad-hoc invitation (Darkstar), no protocol for removal, no protocol for member sabbatical. The roster is whoever is currently present.
- **No archive.** The team keeps no institutional memory, no after-action reports, no lessons-learned. Each engagement is a standalone.
- **No exit doctrine.** No member, across the run, articulates the conditions under which they would leave. The team does not know how to end, which is why, when ended, it ends by entropy rather than by decision.
- **No anniversary.** The team does not mark its own duration. There is no first-year acknowledgment, no founding-day ritual, no commemoration. The unit exists in a flat temporal field.

The time-theory means the Champions cannot learn, cannot mature, and cannot choose to conclude. They can only continue or stop. They stop.

---

## 11. THE OPERATIONAL CODE, COMPRESSED

If one were to extract the Champions' working code into a list — the list the team would never have written — it would read approximately:

1. *Be where ordinary people are. That is enough warrant.*
2. *Wait to be needed. Do not look for trouble; trouble finds the legitimate.*
3. *When called, arrive in force. Hercules first.*
4. *Trust whoever fights beside you. Do not ask what they did before.*
5. *Authority is for people who do not love each other. Decide together; defer to whoever speaks last and loudest.*
6. *Money is Warren's. Force is Hercules's. Spine is Natasha's. Spectacle is Johnny's. Heart is Bobby's. Do not redistribute these.*
7. *Do not call the Avengers. Do not call SHIELD. Do not call the X-Men. We are the alternative; calling would be becoming.*
8. *The public is ours. Their criticism is misunderstanding. Their love is confirmation. Their indifference does not exist.*
9. *Pain is private. Mission is shared. Do not ask each other to bleed twice.*
10. *We will know who we are by what we do. Until then, do.*
11. *We are forever. Do not discuss the alternative.*

Every one of these is, in isolation, recognizable as a virtue. Held together, they describe a team that **cannot prevent, cannot codify, cannot grieve, cannot grow, and cannot end on its own terms** — a team whose worldview is, line for line, the precise inverse of the institutional architecture that would have let it survive. The Champions believed, sincerely and to the end, that sincerity itself would carry them. The sincerity was real. It was not, alone, enough.

— UATU, observing.


## Moral & Ethical Framework

# 6. MORAL & ETHICAL FRAMEWORK — THE CHAMPIONS, AS ENACTED

> "We are the people's champions." — Warren Worthington III, press conference, *Champions* #7
>
> What follows is the audit of that sentence against the behavioral record.

---

## 6.1 The Professed Code (what the team says it is)

The Champions' stated ethical platform, assembled from Warren's press statements (#7), the team's incorporation paperwork referenced across the run, and recurrent in-panel declarations, reduces to five professions:

1. **Accessibility.** "A super-team for the common man" — heroes ordinary citizens can reach, contra the perceived inaccessibility of the Avengers and the secrecy of the X-Men.
2. **Non-establishment posture.** No government contracts, no SHIELD liaison desk, no police coordination by default. The team will *cooperate* with authorities but not *answer* to them.
3. **Open membership.** No mutant exclusion, no nationality test, no metaphysical screen — explicitly demonstrated by the rapid admission of Darkstar (#7), a defecting Soviet asset, with effectively zero security delay.
4. **Proportional force.** No lethal protocol stated, no kill-orders countenanced; the team operates within the standard Code-era Marvel restraint envelope.
5. **Local fidelity.** Los Angeles first. The team frames itself as *of* a place — a civic body, not a global asset.

This is the manifesto. It is the document against which the *enacted* code must be read.

---

## 6.2 The Enacted Code (what the team actually does, derived from cases)

### 6.2.1 Force discipline

In practice, the Champions are a **high-restraint, low-strategy** unit on lethality, with one structural exception. Across 17 issues, no member kills a sapient opponent who could be subdued. Hercules pulls strikes against human-scaled foes with measurable consistency; Iceman ices joints and weapons rather than lungs; Angel's flight is used for extraction at least as often as for offense; Romanoff uses the Widow's Bite at stun settings and gas grenades over firearms.

The exception is **Ghost Rider**, whose Penance Stare and hellfire are not within Blaze's volitional control during Zarathos episodes. The team has no protocol for this. The enacted rule that emerges, *visible only by what they do not say*, is: *we will tolerate one teammate's potentially lethal, dissociative output as the price of his membership*. This is a real ethical decision, made by silence, every issue.

The deeper read: the team is restrained because its individual members are restrained, not because the team has *agreed* to be restrained. Remove any one member and substitute a more aggressive operator (e.g., a Wolverine-grade roster slot) and there is no institutional brake. The force discipline is **personnel-contingent, not doctrinal**.

### 6.2.2 Consent and bystander handling

The Champions are unusually good at evacuation and unusually bad at follow-up. Panel-counted across the run, civilians are physically moved out of harm's way in approximately every engagement (Angel's lift capacity and Iceman's slides are used as ad hoc transit infrastructure). What is *not* done is the post-incident work: no insurance liaison, no displaced-resident shelter, no on-scene medical handoff beyond "the paramedics are coming." Warren's money funds the headquarters and the publicist; it does not, on-panel, fund a victims' fund.

The enacted rule: *we will save your life and leave your living room burning*. This is consistent with the team's broader pattern of operational tempo as a substitute for follow-through — a moral version of the avoidance defense diagnosed earlier.

### 6.2.3 Authority and jurisdiction

The Champions' anti-establishment posture is enacted as **selective non-cooperation**. They will brief LAPD on outcomes; they will not accept tasking. They cooperate with the Avengers when an Avenger is personally present (Iron Man courtesy calls; Hawkeye's brief overlapping operations) but refuse standing liaison. They accept Warren's private funding without scrutinizing its sources, while rejecting public funding that would come with oversight.

The unspoken consistency here is **freedom from accountability, framed as freedom from corruption**. The team mistakes the absence of an oversight body for ethical purity. Romanoff, of all members, would know better; her silence on this is one of the clearer signals that she does not consider the team a serious institutional project, only a transitional one.

### 6.2.4 Membership ethics

The Darkstar admission is the cleanest test case and the team fails it ethically while passing it humanely. A defecting Soviet operative is welcomed with no debrief, no quarantine, no consideration of what the KGB will do in response, and no risk-disclosure to the rest of the roster. The *humane* impulse — receive the defector, do not return her — is correct. The *ethical* execution — do so without protecting your other members from foreseeable blowback — is negligent.

The enacted rule reads: *we will extend membership on the basis of present-tense need, with no diligence on past or future*. Compare the X-Men's (later, admittedly) handling of Rogue and Magneto: contested, debated, ratified or rejected with members on record. The Champions ratify by drift.

### 6.2.5 Truth-telling and disclosure

The team is publicly truthful and internally opaque. Warren does not lie to the press. Romanoff does not brief the team on her threat assessments. Blaze does not disclose the full mechanics of his condition. Hercules does not explain the Olympian political situation that drives several of his engagements. Bobby does not — because he himself does not yet know — name his own discomforts.

Enacted rule: *public candor, private compartmentalization*. The asymmetry is the diagnostic. A genuinely ethical team aligns its internal and external disclosure norms; the Champions do not, and the cost is paid in the Rampage arc and the Sentinels arc, both of which involve threats Romanoff plausibly could have flagged earlier had the team had a working intelligence sharing norm.

### 6.2.6 Lethality of opponents and the "monster" exemption

Against non-human or metaphysical adversaries — Pluto, Ares, Typhon, the Stranger, Swarm, the Brotherhood-of-Evil-Mutants-adjacent threats — the team's restraint envelope quietly expands. Hercules will *throw* a Titan with intent to maim. Ghost Rider will Stare a demon without anyone objecting. The implicit rule is **scaled moral status**: the more "monstrous" the adversary, the less the team's restraint applies.

This is the team's most consistent moral fracture and the one its members would be least comfortable naming. It is not unique to the Champions — most Code-era Marvel teams operate this gradient — but the Champions are exposed because their stated mandate ("for the common man") implies a *universalist* ethics that the scaled-monster gradient contradicts.

---

## 6.3 Where the Code Is Consistent
